British Generals Under
Lord Roberts Uniting
in a Movement That
Places the Republican
Forces in a Difficult
Position.
Joubert Is Now Concentrating His Forces
Thirty-Five Miles North of Bloemfon
tein, Where He May Make an Effort to
Check the Advance of the Imperial In
vaders.
? â â
LONDON, March 16. â Lord Roberts has already started
his movement to crush between the forces of Generals
Clements, Gatacre and Brabant and his own army the
Boers who have held the line of the Orange River for many
months. General Gatacre has crossed the Orange River at
Bethulie and simultaneously General Pole-Carew, with the
Guards Brigade, two guns and mounted infantry, has been sent
south from the Free State capital by railway. He was at Beth
any, twenty miles below Bloemfontein, yesterday. The object
of this move is to secure for Lord Roberts' army control of the
railway between Bloemfontein and Cape Colony, which will im
mensely shorten the British lines of communication.
The Boers are in a difficult position. If they fight General
Clements is ready to advance from Xorvals Pont, General Bra
bant from Aliwal North and General Gatacre from Bethulie,
while General Pole-Carew will be threatening their rear. .
Lord Roberts is evidently under the impression that his
force is ample to meet any resistance which he is likely to en
counter in the Free State, as General Warren's division has been
ordered back from Durban to rejoin General Buller.
The Boers have been locatted in several strong positions
close to the junction of Drakensberg and Biggersberg Range,
from which it would be impossible for General Buller. acting
alone, to drive them without heavy loss of life and a repetition
of the terrible encounters among the hills of the Tugela. But
with Lord Roberts moving north along, the. .railway General
Joubert will be obliged to divide his forces. Should he suffer
defeat at the hands of the commander in chief General Buller
would then seize the opportunity to advance.
General Joubert is now concentrating his forces at Brand
fort, thirty-five miles north of Bloemfontein, where he may
make an effort to check the British.
All the dispatches to the London newspapers describe at
great length the enthusiastic welcome given to the British
troops by the inhabitants of Bloemfontein. It should be borne
in mind, however, that a large percentage of the residents of
the Free State capital are English, most of whom remained
there after war was declared.

BRITONS SATISFIED WITH THE CAMPAIGN.
LONDON". March 16, 5 a. m.â Events are moving In the South African cam
paign In a epwdy and satisfactory manner from both the military and political
points of view. It could hardly have been expected by the most sanguine Eng
lishmen that they would take a turn so favorable.
It appears that when Major Weston cut the railway north of Bloemfontein
he thereby Intercepted General Joubert, who. far from having retired from the
campaign, was then coming southward with 3000 men. presumably to superintend
the defense. Elaborate defense works three miles long had been prepared out
side the town.
No Boer wounded were left In Bloemfontein. When asked this reason by Lord
Roberts'. Mr. Frazer replied: "The burghers do not like fish and would not care
to co to Cape Town." V '.â¢." : ...- *
The evidence goes to Fhow that, so far as the southern part of the Free State
is concerned, there will be no further resistance. It is understood that Mr.
Frazer. Mr. Wessels and other leaders are quite prepared to accept the position
of a pelf-governing British colony. ";".â¢Â¦
The cheers which greeted the reading in Parliament yesterday of the corre
spondence with the United States are re-echoed throughout the country. The
ppeech of M. Delcasse has Increased this satisfaction and, taken with the general
belief that Emperor Nicholas Is personally averse to any intervention, these inci
dents spread the Jdea that there will be no further serious attempt to interfere
from any quart' r.
Mr. Montagu White's threat. In an American newspaper, that the Boera will
cacrifice Johannesburg and raze It to the ground if necessary, is not taken very
seriously. Mr. Chamberlain's statement that President Kruger has already
been warned as to the consequence of such conduct. Is regarded as showing that
sufficient precaution has been taken. The Times suggests that the burghers
should be warned that their farms will be taxed as a guarantee against any dam
age to British property, but no serious apprehensions are entertained of such con
duct as 3ux. White is said to have foreshadowed. J. B. Robinson, the milllion
alre mlne'owner, says he does not believe the Boers would be so foolish.
By the time Lord Roberts reaches the Vaal River he will command some 80 -
000 men. while General Buller will have 40,000. From the military po!-t of view
the critics now think there Is nothing to fear.
CAMPAIGN AGAINST BOERS ON THE
LINE OF ORANGE RIVER IS BEGUN
VOLUME LXXXVII â NO. 106.
PRICE FIVE CENTS.
SAX FRANCISCO, FRIDAY, MARCH 16, 1900.
f? ONDON, March 16. â Spencer Wilkinson, in the Morning
Post, commenting upon the very satisfactory nature. of the
==^ occupancy of Bloemfontein, says: "The anticipation that the
Boer resistance along the Orange River would collapse on British
occupation of the Free State capital seems to have been realized.
It will be interesting to await the meeting and perhaps the fight
between the British troops moving southward on the railway/and
the retiring Boers.
"Looking to the strength of Lord Roberts' army by the time
he reaches Vaal River, it is not surprising that Sir Charles War
ren's division is retained in Natal. An advance of the two armies
on separata lines will be quite the best form of operations to crush
the enemy's forces and to bring about his absolute submission.
"The Boers cannot longer afford to divide their forces. Serious
resistance in Natal would enable Lord Roberts to. cross the Vaal
and compromise the Boer force in Natal. Serious resistance at
Brandfort or Ventersberg would enable General Buller to pass the
mountains and threaten the Boer communications."

f>ines, Hawaii and Porto Rico. The board
s to assemble In Washington once every
month. Its action will not of course be
final, but it will make recommendations to
Secretary Long, who, in view cf the
weighty chaiacter of the advice which
will be offered, will undoubtedly approve
them. There Is a disposition on the part
of naval officers to believe that the
board's duties will tfe eventually enlarged
until It will consider all Important naval

scavenger carts are permitted to
menace the health of San Fran
cisco. * Every day these receptacles
of reeking filth, these depositories
of bandages from eick rooms and hos
pitals, of accumulated slim** arid putrefac
tion. pa.Â«s freely througrh the city's most
densely thronged streets when business
compels thousands to be imperiled. The
(senses revolt at the smell of decaying
vegetation and the winds sweep millions
of germs of disease into stores, hotels,
Fchoolhousos and dwellings and taint the
air that the multitude must breathe.
There are closed carts for certain classes
of refuse; but there are many more that
are not closed. They are in motion con
etantly from sunrise until sunset. They
pass along Market street, Kearny street.
Mission street, Howard street, Folsom
street and all other principal thorough
fares of the city. Seventh street is the
scene of continual garbage menace. On
that street carts ; are gathered from all
sections of "the city, going to or return
ing from the garbage crematory, and the
winds have opportunity to disperse the
most deadly part of their contents, the
disease germs. They are covered not at
all or so flinisily covered that they lose a
part of their loads on the journey.
This is also true in ail the streets
through which they pass. It is particu
larly true of all the residence sections,
where the children of the poor and of
the rich are alike needlessly endangered.
While the Phelanized Board or Health
i.- urovt-ling in the search for bubonic
jplapue to warrant a raid on the treasury
of the city and has been guilty of false
pretenses to fool the public a danger a
thousand times more to be feared than
the one that reached its official apotheosis
in the assassination of a guinea pig. and
a monkey is apparent to all observers.
But no member of the Board of Health
raiHf-s his voice to call a halt; no ap
potntÂ«e of Phelan braves the anger of his
official master pointing out that there
is an easy way to escape evident ills.
This is to have the garbage collected and
transported when the- people are not
stirrir:^; when the thousands of school
children are not on the streets or within
reach of possible infection. The Board
of Supervisors has the power to regulate
tli<- maner. Â«
If any modern scientific fact is estab
lished it is that germs account-for a great
variety of diseases and that the air car
ries the germs and Is, therefore, when it
is tainted, a powerful instrument for the
infection of great communities like San
Francisco. Reporters and artists em
ployed by The Call yesterday went on a
lour of inspection. They found open
garbage wagon* in all the principal
streets. Many of these were permitted to
stand for a long time in front of stores
and rr-s'iienct-s. entirely uncovered, with
the heat of the sun pouring down upon
them and with the wind blowing their
contents about. Crowds of people passed
an<l repasst-<l. Groups of children sported
near by. .Many pictures were taken, Â¦of
which a few are published this morning.
In all parts of the city the conditions ob
j served were practically the same.
On Kearny street. In the center of the
chopping district, in the middle of the
day, when many persons lined thp thor
oughfare, stood a scavenger cart filled to
overflowing. The unsavory contents
towered so high that the scavengers were
compelled to get on top of the load and
trample it down to keep it from entirely
blowing away.
On Folsom street two scavengers ha<l
a mixf-d mass of stuff to deal with, which
they had to level off with forks. The
odors emanating therefrom caused all
fiassers to hold their noses. Directly op
posite was Columbia Square, with hun
dreds of little children at play. In many
i.laces there were crowds of children near
Ihe reeking: scavenger carts and the pic
tures presented indicate the populous con
dition of some of the neighborhoods where
the views were taken.
. No first-class city deals with the matter
of health in this reckless fashion with the
exception of San Francisco. Amonp the
riiseases known to originate from germs
are th<Â» following: Tetanup, tuberculosis,
symptomatic anthrax, malaria, diphtheria,
glanders and rinderpest.
The Board of Health, falling to look af
\fr the collection of garbage under con
ditions threatening the least danger, de

TURKS TROUBLED
BY RUSSIA'S MOVES
IN ASIA MINOR
Government Advises Against Submission
and Sultan Awaits a Military Report on
Strategic Advantages*
Russian Press Treats the Matter in a Conservative
Way and Hints That Preparations for Man
euvers Were Misconstrued.
f*\ ONSTANTINOPLE, March 13.â There is great anxiety in official circles
I here regarding the Russian Government's refusal to modify its demands
1 regarding railroad concessions in Asia Minor. The Turkish Government
I . advises against submission and the Sultan is awaiting a military report
on the strategical aspect of the question before giving a decision.
LONDON, March 16.â The Constantinople correspondent of the Times says:
"There is a rumor that Russia Intends to ask Turkey for a coaling station in the
archipelago, either in Lemnos, Imbros or Mltylene."
The St. Petersburg correspondent of the Times says: "Fresh alarm is spread
ing abroad from South Russia with regard to further suspicious military move
ments. Russian troops are being concentrated at Odessa. Perhaps, however,
it is only in timely preparation for the grand maneuvers."
SHOT DEAD BEFORE
HER FATHER'S EYES
Young Woman Murdered and a Jury
Exonerates the Parent for
Avenging the Deed.
ST. PAUL, Minn., March 15.â A Butte,
Mont., special to the Pioneer Press says:
A tragedy at Bannock yesterday, reports
of which have Just been received, resulted
in the death of Fred Brown and Myrtle
Lyttle, the daughter of William Lyttle.
There was hard feelings between the fam
ilies. Last Tuesday Brown told Lyttle he
would bring his pun up to Lyttle's house
and straighten this thing up. Wednesday
morning about 11 o'clock Brown ap
proached Lyttle's house. Myrtle Lyttle
went to meet him and was seen "to talk
with him. He said he intended to "kill
the whole outfit." She turned to go into
the house, when Brown shot her in the
back, delivering a shot after she fell. Lyt
tle heard the shots, ran to the door with
a gun and seeing his daughter lying on the
ground fired at Brown with deadly effect
killing him Instantly. Lyttle surrendered.
The Coroner's jury returned a verdict of
justifiable homicide.

WOULD GIVE CONGRESS
CONTROL OF ALL TRUSTS
Ray of New York Offers a Sweeping
Amendment to the National
Constitution,
( WASHINGTON', March 15.â Representative Ray of New York, chairman of
the House Committee on Judiciary and at the head of the special sub-com
mittee on trusts, to-day introduced the following joint resolution proposing
an amendment to the constitution:
Resolved, etc.. That the following article be proposed to the Legislatures of the
several States as an amendment to the constitution of the United States:
Article XVlâ The Congress of the United States shall have power to repress
monopolies and combinations; to create and dissolve corporations and dispose of their
property; to make all laws necessary and proper for the execution of the foregoing
powers. Such powers may be exercised by the several States in any manner not in
conflict with the laws of the United States.
GREEKS OF OLO
AND THEIR GODS
ABLY DISCUSSES
President "Wheeler on the
Unique Religion of the
Ancients.
BERKELEY, March 15.â President Ben
jamin Ide Wheeler of the University of
California delivered an address on "Greek
Religion in Greek Art" before the Art
Association of the University of Califor
nia in Hearst Hall this evening. The lec
ture was illustrated by stereopticon views
of the buildings and points of interest in
Greece as developed through the excava
tiors during^'the past ten years.
Dr. Wheeler showed that the Greeks
made thf ir religion pre-eminent. All livAr
wonderful architectural monuments were
developed by their religious instincts. "No
j>eople was ever great," he said, "without
a religion. The religion of the Greeks was
omnipresent. Everything centered on re
ligion. His entire environment and lift;
was religious. The voice of God was in
everything. Unlike tlie Semetic God,
which is relegated to a distance and be
fore waom all bow, the Greek god was on
a par with his worshipers. The god wai
sought m 1m 1 dwellings and made one of the
family.
"The Greek showed respect to the gods
as fellow members cf the state and as
chief citizer.3 of the state. The state was
a. socio-rtiigious bond. Religion was not
,a matter of creeds and theologies, but
fellowship. To the Greek impiety was
treason and treason impiety. Any one
who offended any of the gx>ds created a
treason against the state.
"In the summer the gods in all their
plorv were entertained in the best style
the Greeks knew. As athletics was thÂ«*
life it was made the entertainment for
thf g-ods at their festivals."
Dr. Wheeler made a comparison between
the old Greek religion and that of to-day.
The Greek was on familiar terms, he
Bald, with his gods. He asked favors of
th^m as he would of a friend. He did not
bos- down to the last to something he
could not imagine. They had no devil as
we understand it. he said, and no god
lift*-*! up. but they always had the god
with them. Evil to their minds always
came mixed up with God in the personal
ity. Ti.rre is no word in Greek that means
good. There is no such thing as good. A
man may be just, but is never cailed
good. There were no good men. and,
above a. I. none who were cailed goody
goody. There, of course, came a time
when Gr.-ece tired of philosophy and a
time whr-ri they made A. distinction be
tween good and bad an we know it. Then
came the iirst intimation of sin. They, un
like us, put their gods on the stage nnd
threw ridicule at them. Gods like Apollo
which brought plenty to the land were
never made mirth of.
Views of the Acropolis, Parthenon and
familiar historical points of Greece, and
Athens particularly. werÂ» thrown on the
screen and described by President Wneel
cr. The beautiful Parthenon, with Its
wonderful cells, was minutely described.
"The builders of these magnificent tem
ples were no ordinary workmen," said he.
"They were of those whose equal for
alertness and architectural ability tb-s
world has never known."

epent two years In exploring and exca-

WAR AGAINST GAMBLERS.
Man Charged With Selling Fools Ar
rested in Yolo County.
Special Dispatch to The Call
WOODLAND, March 13.â The ramblers
recently driven out of Sacramento and
who opened poolrooms in Washington will
not be granted immunity in Tolo County.
On information furnished by men who
watched agents of theirs purchase pools
two John Doe warrants were issued to
day and Deputy Sheriff Henry Bucking
ham of Washington arrested J. H., Davis
and brought him to this city on z charge
of pool selling to await his trial. Frank
Daroux came over from Sacramento and
gave security for Davis' appearance. Da
roux says he can beat the Yolo ordinance
and District Attorney Eruton says he will
push the matter to a finish and Is confi
dent of winning. The case is likely to go
to the highest court for settlement.
W. H. Vioget Dead.
SAN JOSE. March 15.â W. H. Vloget.
widely known as a breeder and trainer
of trotting horses and also as a breeder
of fine stock of all kinds, died in this
city to-day. He formerly followed the
trotting circuit, but for a number of year*
past had devoted himself to his stock
farm near Laurence station. He was born
in San Francisco fifty-two years ago.
PASSENGER AGENT SHOT
DEAD BY HIS WIFE
She Declares That for Years He Had
Abused Her and Had Threat
ened Her Life. .
CINCINNATI. March la.-Charles Ad
ams, a passenger agent for the Union Pa
cific, who came here with his wife from
Omaha last October with their two little
children, Irving and Fay, was shot and
killed by his wife; Jessie Turman-Adams.
to-night, in the Primrose flat on Race
street. The only person present was Ger
tie Turman, the sister of the wife, who Is
an actress under the name of Gertie
Hays.
Mrs. Adams, when taken to the station
house, said she had been living in a veri
table hell for years; that her husband
threatened her life; that he would hold
her eldest son by the heels, head down
ward, outside of a fifth-story window, and
had been habitually cruel. He had re
cently threatened her life and she had
provided herself with a revolver for self
defense. To-night he opened -up at sup
per time with a terrific tirade of abuse,
followed with a blow across the nose
which felled her to the floor, and then
struck her again. She struggled to her
feet, drew a pistol and fired one shot,
which struck him in the shoulder. He
turned his back and then she fired again.
This time the ball entered behind the
right e?r and came out above the left eye.
It was a fatal shot.
Adams has relatives in Omaha, and the
sisters, Mrs. Adams ami Miss Turman,
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, MARCH 16, 1900.
WITNESS PRODUCED
THAT SLUMPED THE
FAIR ATTORNEYS
Mrs* James Gilleran Tells a Strong Story of
James G* Fair's Admission of Marriage
to Mrs. Nettie Craven.
Placed on the Stand and Subjected to Rigid and
Persistent Cross Examination by McEner
ney She Was Not Entangled.
WHEN Attorney Plerson said
"That is all." after he had
finished with his cross-examin
ation of Mrs. Sarah E. John
sen on Wednesday afternoon,
Lafe Pence, attorney for Mrs. Craven
*a:r, pledged his word to the court that
the attorneys for the petitioner would
Onuh their case on the morrow. The
morrow has come and gone and yet the
oetense is by no means at the end of its
case. Who is at fault is difficult to say.
The last witness of the day was a Mrs.
Janes Gilleran. proprietress of the El
Monte Hotel and formerly the landlady of
the Windsor House. She told a story that
Kept the attorneys for the respondents in
a stew of anxiety. Before adjournment
the had Garret McEnerney, who was in
charge cf the cross-battery fire yesterday
afternoon, in a cold perspiration and
floundering in a sea of confusion.
Never was a witnessâ and a woman at
thatâ more sure of her ground. Trippingly
from her tongue came the story of James
G. Fair's proclamation of the wifehood
cf Mrs. Nettie Craven. With aÂ»good run
r.inj; start McEnerney attempted to beat
tlifr field, but he died at the half-mile post
and reserved the right to further cross
question Monday morning when the case
will be resumed. Mrs. Gilleran left the
stand unperturbed and locke*. "vithin an
armor of apparent truth.
The morning session began with a con
tinuation of the cross-examination of H.
M. Bryant, the man of many homes. At
torney Pierson subjected him to a verbal
gattUas pun fire with, the result that
Bryant was gasping for breath before the
finish. Hardly had the trouble begun be
fore McEnerney. in answer to an objection
of one of the Pence brotherhood, jumped
to his feet and stridently shouted:
"We think the testimony of this wit
ness < s a fabrication and we wish to show
That the declaration that this man gave
$50 to jj U ne Payne, a woman whom he
knew but a week, is preposterous."
"'lf you work along those lines," retort
ed Charles Pence, "we might show sev
eral instances where attorneysâ and I
mention no names â of the c*Â»posite side
have given much more on a thorter ac- '
quaintance."
The cross-examination brought out that
Errant was any kind of a man in regard
to occupation. He delivered lectures on
the labor question, financial problems; '
was the founder of a Grand Army post; j
had serred four years in the war of the
rebellion; was an employe of the Santa Â¦
Ke Railroad and manager of the Pacific j
Grove Bank. His testimony concluded
with the recital of a scheme to secure his
signature to a paper on Wednesday even
ing at his Oakland home by some un
known party, who represented himself to
be an officer serving a subpoena.
"He was a big, corpulent man with a
black mustache, but I fooled him." eaid
Bryant.
"Ti-at's all." said Piexson, and Bryant
vent his way subject to recall.
Louis Whitcomb, reporter of the Chron
icle, was the next witness called. Charles
Pence claimed that the story printed In
that paper yesterday morning was a slur
end a bitter attack on the attorneys for
Mrs. Craven-Fair and likewise a contempt
of court. McEnerney objected to the ex
amination of Whitcomb, as it was a mat
ter extraneous to the case. Some hot !
argument ensued, with the finality that j
Whltcvmb was allowed to resume his re- I
portorial labors just as the trial was ad
journed until the afternoon session.
The matinee battle began by calling
.for several witnesses who did not put in
an appearance. The bailiff cried loud and
lustily for Mrs. Kate C. Plumber, but she
was not on deck to answer. Reuben H.
Lloyd was wanted by the Pcnces, and he,
too, was not within reach, so they quietly
announced that Mrs. James Gilleran
would be the next witness.
Mrs. Gilleran proved the best witness
for the petitioner's cause since the trial
began. She is a woman of strong Hiber
nian accent, with a positiveness of man
ner and speech that admitted of no bom
bardment. In her capacity as landlady
of the Windsor House she said that she
had seen Senator Fair In the rooms of
Mrs. Craven, who was stopping in her
house in July and August of 1592. On one
occasion when she was in the room to
drape a mantel lambrequin Mrs. Craven
and Senator Fair were present. Mrs.
Craven and Mrs. Gilleran were working
over the mantel decoration and Senator
Fair was sitting on the sofa when the fol
lowing conversation ensued:
"Are you married to him?" said Mrs.
Gilleran.
Mrs. Craven then turned to Senator Fair
and said:
"Did you hear that. James? She wants
to know if we are married."
"What's that?" responded the Senator,
and when the question was repeated by
Mrs. Craven he replied, laughingly: "XVs,
we are, and why shouldn't we be? My
children have all gone back on me nnd
Mrs. Craven is a good woman. Didn't I
do right?" And then Pence turned th*
witness over to McEnerney, who conduct
ed the cross-examination.
For two hours that legal luminary kept
Mrs. Gilleran on the prill, eliciting a
wealth of testimony that strengthened the
petitioner's side of thÂ« case. She was
a "hard customer" and McEnerney got
the worst of the bargain. Question after
question, thrown at her with expected de
structive force was repelled with an in
stinctive wit that made the attorneys of
the respondents perspire mentally. Mrs.
Gilleran told of the visits of Fair to Mrs.
Craven in the Windsor House: of a meet
ing on Montgomery street when the pe
titioner and the Senator, arm in arm.
walked lovingly over the pavements; of
money sent through herself to Mrs. Cra
ven, who was stopping at the California
Hotel, and much other Interesting stuff
about the close association of the two.
"I saw him go In her room at least
twenty times," said Mrs. Gilleran. and
she recalled the individual meetings with
great exactness.
Badgered as she was by McEnerney the
witness did not display any confusion or
ill temper. When asked if she had signed
anj- contract for recompense for testify
ing she indignantly remarked that Mr.
McEnerney knew her too well for that.
The attorney said he did not. This aroused
Mrs. Gilleran and she related the visits
of Judge Lawlor. Thomas Flynn and Mc-
Ererney himself.
Disclaiming: any interest in the trial it
self Mrs. Gilleran said it was against her
will that she was called in to testify.
"I foolishly remarked before Senator
Morehouse. his wife and daughter. Dr.
Reickart and Dr. Bardu at my hotel, the
El Monte, that I knew of the marriage of
Senator Fair and Mrs. Craven. That was
the mistake. Soon after I was visited by
the Pence?, but I would not be inter
viewed. Then I was subpenaed and here
I am."
McEnerney persistently fired away at
the witness. She admitted an acquaint
ance with Martin Kelly; but disclaimed
any connection with him in regard to this
case. She said that she had spoken of her
knowledge about the relationship of Mrs.
Craven and Senator Fair to but very* few.
At this point the hour for adjournment
had arrived and Judge Troutt called the
case off until Monday, when Attorneys
Plerson and McEnerney will risk another
sheaf of shafts at the buxom landlady.

SHARP DEBATE
OVER THE BOLL
PEN INQUIRY
First Witness on Behalf
of the Defense
Testifies.
Refuses to Answer a Question Put by
Lentz Which Is Characterized.
by Stevens as Absurd and
Nonsensical.
WASHINGTON, March 15.â Although
the direct testimony In the Coeur d'Alene
investigation before the House Commit
tee on Military Affairs Is not yet closed,
the prosecuting witnesses temporarily
gave place to-day to. the first witness not
identilied with those bringing ,the charges.
This was J. H. Forney, the special prose
cuting officer, who directed the cases be
fore the Coroner's jury and the Grand
Jury at the seat of the disorder. He said,
he had been a prosecuting official for sev-.
enteen years, and. was especially ap
pointed in this case by the Attorney Gen
eral of Idaho.
The witness said the Coroner's investi
gation, which was much criticized on the
direct examination, was conducted in the
usual way and in accordance withy the
law. The jury not only investigated the
deaths resulting from the riot of April 29,
but also the conspiracy leading up to that
demonstration. The theory that a con
spiracy existed gave the inquiry a broad
scope. Mr. Forney said, however, that no
force, violence or intimidation was used
toward the witnesses. Concerning the
prisoners in the "bull pen," Mr. Forney
said they were not held under any specific
charges,- but by virtue of the Governor's
proclamation declaring that the county
was in a state of insurrection.
This was in accordance with the law,
he said, which provided that In case of
insurrection the Governor may appoint a
special officer with an armed force to take
charge of affairs.
The witness was examined at length on
thÂ» legal rights involving the suspension
of the writ of habeas carpus, etc. He said
the writ- had not been suspended In Idaho
although the action of the. Governor and
the holdings of the courts might have had
that practical effect. He maintained,
however, that the denial of a writ of ha
beas corpus in particular cases did -not
operate as a general suspension, of 'the
system. The cross-examintlon by Lentz
and Sulzer was directed to show that
the Attorney General of Idaho demurred
to the application of habeas corpus and
In effect held that the writ was sus
pended.
A heated controversy arose over the hy
pothetical .Question by Lentz as to
whether if a sister of charity: had been
put in the "bull pen" she could have se
cured a writ of habeas corpus. Â¦' â¢ Â¦ '
The answer was that no such condition
could have existed. Â¦â¢ Â¦Â¦Â¦ Â¦ . ?:- --. ;
When Lentz pressed the question, Stev
ens of Minnesota sharply- protested that
the question was "absurd and nonsensi
cal." " 'Â¦-;: : . Â¦ - *
Lentz declared the witness was seeking
to evade. the question, whereupon the wit
ness turned to the Ohio member. and said:
"I am perfectly honest In this matter, I
\jant you to understand, sir."
The witness finally declined to 1 answer I
the Question further. v - J
Representative Hay of Virginia raised
the point that this placed the witness In
contempt, and asked for a vote on hold-
Ing the witness to be in contempt.
A controversy of half an hour followed,
bringing frequent sharp and rather per
sonal exchanges between Marsh of Illi
nois and Lentz of Ohio.. The question was
finally changed and the controversy
closed.
When the witness was asked as to his
politics Jett of Illinois Inquired if the in
vestigation was to be on political lines.
Marsh answered that in his opinion the
Investigation was conceived and executed
for political purposes. Â¦ >
General Merriam's Denials.
WASHINGTON,. March 15.â Brigadier

TRAMPS ROB A STORE.
Arrested by, Officers and the Stolen
Goods Recovered.
CORNING, March 15.â A party of tramps
gained entrance to B. J. Morey's bicycle
and jewelry store at this place at about 9
o'clock last night and succeeded In carry-
Ing off a large number of watches, rings
and other articles. Mr. Mbrey was ab
sent from his stcre at the time and did not
become aware of the robbery until an
hour or so after the thieves had departed.
The officers were notified and a search
at once began for the robbers. Constable
W. Hart succeeded in finding them near
Tehama. He also recovered nineteen of
the stolen watches. After daybreak this
morning a more thorough hunt was made
for the rest of the stolen goods. Three
watches and seventeen rings were found
near the place where the arrest was made.
The prisoners are now In jail at this place
awaiting trial. The same gang of tramps
resisted arrest at Orland a day previous
to their arrival in Corning, overpowered
the constable of that place, handcuffed
his hands behind his back and pinned his
star on the back of his coat. The evi
dence of their guilt is so conclusive that
a speedy conviction is assured.

Brwlal Cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyright, ISOO, by the New York
Herald Comx-any.
PANAMA. March 16.â The United States
Canal Commission yesterday again visited
the Gorgona section of the Panama canal,
where they embarked on the canal com
pany's 'tugs. They went down the Cha
jrres River to Bohio and returned from
that place to Colon to-day. They came
over to inspect the company's hospitals.
The Scorpion will return to Caledonia
Bay to-morrow.

FOUR REPORTS ON THE
PACIFIC CABLE BILL
Committee Divided as to the Advisa
bility of Permitting Private
Construction of the Line.
WASHINGTON. March 15.â Four dis
tinct reports on the bill for the construc
tion of a Pacific cable to Hawaii, the
Philippines and Japan were filed to-day
from membors of the House Committee
on interMPte and Foreign Commerce.
Sherman of New York submitted the-ma
jority report favorable to the bill intro
duced by him for a cable to be built under
private auspices, the Government paying
RWti.OOO annually for twenty years tor thi
transmission of Government messages
A minority report, signed by Represent
atives Adamson. Adams, Shackleford and
Davey. dissents in toto from the bill. An
other minority report signed by Repre
sent atives Corliss, Fletcher and Stewart
opposed the plan of private ownershij
and proposed a substitute embodying the
Idea of Government ownership of thÂ«,
cable. Still another dissenting report is
from Mann of Illinois, giving his individ
ual view that a Government cable woula
prove more beneficial to the public and
the Government.
Sherman'f majority report is an ex
haustive review of the cable question It
fays that the argument for a Government
cabkÂ« thut would be under the complete
control of the I'nited States seems of
fmall Importance to the committee except
in time rt war. and in that event the.
Government Is authorized to assume con
trol of the private cable which the com
mittee recommends.
As to the Government cable being more
economical to the I'nited States the re
port says that this loses weight from the
necessity of establishing a duplicate
cable.

MURDERER WILCOX SAYS
HE EXPECTS TO HANG
Body of His Wife Will Be Disinterred
. for a New Examination of the
Wounds.
Special Dispatch to The Call. *
SANTA ROSA. March 15.-George F.
Wllcoxj the felf-cbnfessed wife murderer,
now occupies a cot In his cell at the Coun
ty Jail and is either feigning illness or is
a very sick man. He seems to have given
up hope and admitted to a Call represent
ative to-day Â¦ that he expected to swing
for his crime.
In his statement yesterday Wilcox said
that he shot his wife three timea, detail
ing with great care the exact spot at
which the. bullets entered her prostrate
form. 'The testimony introduced before
the Coroner's jury was that on examina
tion the body had revealed buf one gun
shot wound. "; Â» :.-â¢â¢ .5-
This morning "Wilcox was taken from
hl3 cell and conducted to Department 2
of the Superior Court, where he was ar
raigned. His plea was set for to-morrow
morning at 10 o'clock, j Wllcox's father,
who resides at Pasadena, has arrived here
and will do what he can to assist his son.
It is understood that Attorney Emmet
Seawell has been retained to appear for
the defense.
In discussing the matter to-day the old
man said: "My boy fairly worshiped his
wife and I know that he would not have
harmed a hair of her head for the world
if he had been in his right mind. He
loved. her too well for that. - The boy is
sick, and even If he escapes punishment
will not have long to live. He has been
failing very fast ever since a severe at
tack of typhoid fever which, visited htm
last wlntfr."
Sheriff Grace is in somewhat of a quan
dary what to do In the matter of the $100
reward offered for Wllcox's capture. Two
people claim the reward. One of them Is
the prisoner's stepmother, who fays she
notified the Pasadena authorities as soon
as Wilcox arrived at' his father's home.
The other Is Marshal ' Lacy of Pasadena,
who made the arrest. The sum will prob
ably be divided between the two claim
ants. "

THREATEIS THE
POLICY OF THE
"OPEN DOOR"
Attitude of the Empress
Viewed With Much
Alarm.
Warship From Manila Will Be Dis
patched to Subdue Attacks on
Missionaries by Chinese
Anti-Foreijjn League.
SHANGHAI, March 15.â The American
Association here to-day telegraphed to
the I'nited States Government that the
attitude of the Empress Dowager toward
the reformers will upset the '"open door"
policy. They also say rebellion and
anarchy are expected to the detriment of
foreign internets, and advocate prompt
concerted action on the part of the
powers.
The China Association will appeal to the
liritish Minister, Sir Claude M. Macdon
ala. on the came subject,
WASHINGTON, March 15.â The cable
gram from the American Association has
Uven received at the State Department.
It has not yet been acted upon, but is un
der serious consideration. At tirst read
ing it is not clear just what connection
exists between the attitude of the Chi
nese Empress Dowager toward the re
form element lv China and the "open
door' policy, in which the United States,
with tiie remainder of the civilized world,
is interested. It is recalled, however, that
the Empress Dowager is persecuting with
irleiulet-s hatred the reform party, going
Â«= o far as to resurt to the baroarous prac
tice of offering a large sum of money foi
the assassination of leading reformers.
This reJorm element in China is believed
to include nearly all of the Chinese who
are disposed to elevate China into a civ
ilized nation, following in the footsteps
of Japan. They realize that this can only
be done with the help of foreigners, and
tre theretore disposec to the "open door"
as one of the measures to liberalize and
enlighten China through the introduction
of foreign capital and foreign methods.
It Is assumed that the basis of this repre
sentation from the American Association
is an apprehension that the Conservative
party surrounding the Empress Dowager,
in eatisfying their animosity against the
rcfomers, will excite the dormant anti
foreign feeling among the masses of Chi
nese to a degree that will make the "open
door" Impossible of attainment.
Minister Fails to Report.
It is a curious fact that our Minister to
China, Mr. Conger, has so far made no
representations to the State Department
touching this subject. He has reported
to the department that serious attacks
have been made upon the American mis
sions in Shantung by the "Boxers." a
powerful and numerous anti-foreign
league of Chinese. It is this same organi
sation which has attacked some of the
French missionaries in that quarter of
China and hap even carried operations to
the very doors of Kiaochau, the German
holding in North China. Upon the Minis
ter's representation the State Depart
ment ha* decided that an American war
ship fhall be dispatched from the fleet at
Manila to the nearest convenient port to
the seat of discord, probably in this case
Taku. at the mouth of the I'eiho River, or
Wei-H.ii-Wei. It Is expected, however,
that the Chinese Government will be able
to repress the "Boxers" without any ac
tion <m the part of the American warship.
It is distinctly understood that this lit
tle naval demonstration will have no con
nection with the struggle now going on
between the Empress Dowagers party
and the reformers, for this may be regard
ed as a matter of purely internal policy,
with which our Government has no right
to concern itself. It is probable therefore
that this decision on our part indicates
the character of the response that will be
made by the Department of State to the
cablegram from the American Associa
tion.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, MARCH 16, 1900.
NEW WARSHIPS
PROVIDED FOR
BY COMMITTEE
Â¦ ? Â¦ â â
House. Bill Ready Making
an Appropriation of
' $22,623,000.
Two Sea-Going Line
Three Armored Cruisers and
Three Protected Cruisers
Included in the Bill.
WASHINGTON, March 15.â The House!
Committee on Naval Affairs reached a
definite and final decision to-day as to the
number of new warships to be authorized
in the forthcoming: naval appropriation
bill, as follows:
Two sea-going coast line battleships of
about 13,500 ; tons each, to cost approxi
mately $3,C00,000 each;, three armored
cruisers of the highest practical speed and
most powerful armor and armament, to
cost approximately $4,000,000 each; three
protected cruisers, to cost about $1,141,000
each. - _ . â j
It was determined not to provide any
gunboats, in view of the opinion expressed
by Secretary Long and Admiral Dewey !
that General Otis' recent purchases of ser
viceable boats of this character met pres
ent gunboat requirements.
The committee decided to authorize the
Secretary of the Navy to contract tor
armor at a price not to exceed $315 per
ton. This applies tQ the emergency armor,
about 7400 tons, required for the battle
ships Maine, Missouri and Ohio, now k
course of construction, and not to the ves
sels authorized but not begun nor to those
contemplated by the present bill.
The question of sheathing ships, which
has excited much interest in naval circles
of late, was determined by adopting a
provision leaving the question of sheath
ing to .the discretion of the Secretary of
the Navy. Prior to the action on the bill
rsaval Constructor Capps, who served
with Admiral Dewey in the Phllipplres,
was heard on the sheathing question. He
urged In particular that vessels to be u?ed
in foreign service should be sheathed, as
foreign drydocks were not always avail
able. ;
'The committee did not provide specifical
ly for the building of any of the new ships
in Government yards, as has been urged
by delegations from Brooklyn and else
where, so that it is expected that the
usual provisions as to building will be In
serted in the bill, with possibly some dis
cretionary power with the Navy Depart
ment as to utilizing Government yards for
this purpose. ' . .
The decisions of to-day practically com
plete the bill, although there is still a
week's work in' preparing the formal lan
guage executing the determination of the
committee as expressed to-day.

TRYING TO FIND
WHERE WHITTELL
REALLY BESIDES
Residence of the Clubman a
Moot Point Before the
Federal Court.
"Is George Whlttell a resident of San
Francisco or of New York?" is the prob
lem which United States Circuit Judge
Morrow will endeavor to solve wlthm the
next few weeks. He has just finished the
taking of testimony and the reading of
depositions bearing upon that important
Issue. The matter in issue is whether
the Federal court has jurisdiction in the
case of Ellen A. Fife and George S FlrV
her husband, against George Whlttell the
well-known society and club man The
suit was brought some time ago in the
Superior Court, but upon a showing made
by Mr. Whitteil to the effect that he had
shaken the dust of San Francisco off bis
feet the case was removed to the L'nittd
States Circuit Court. Now the plaint*if
come in with the contention that Whitteil
has never abandoned his residence in this
city and that therefore the Federal courts
have no jurisdiction.
The case involves the disposition of the
estate left by the late Nicholas Lumn*
who died on August 11. ISM. leaving an es
tate valued at $7,245,000. and Whlttell and
Marlon B. Langhorne were appointed ex
ecutors.
Mrs. Fife, a daughter of the deceased
and sister of Mrs. Whlttell. alleges tint
the value of her portion of the estate was
$1.200.0j0 In money, stocks and bonds pn-1
$200,000 In real estate, and avers that Whit
tell induced her to transfer all her right
title and Interest to a corporation formed'
by the heirs and called the ,Luning Com
pany. She avers that she was afterward
lnformf-d by Whittell that the value of
her Interest In the company had shrunk to
$700,000. She accordingly asks for a judg
ment for $750.362 as damages.
Mr. Whlttell introduced witnesses before
Judge Morrow to prove that he was a
recognized club man In New York City
and a resident of that place. Dr. W. X
Hopkins told of declaration." made by Mr!
Whittell of his intention to remove to New
York and to become a permanent resident.
Mr. Whittell on cross-examination ad
mitted that he went to New York 'n the
first Instance to avoid service In onÂ» of
the Luning suits, but that he at that time
also intended to make that metropolis his
permanent residence.
Joseph D. Grant told about a conversa
tion that he had had with Whittell i* io
the superior advantages oftereVi by ths
Eastern metropolis for a business mun
with great energy and capital.
Mrs. Fife testified that the Whlttell resi
dence, on the southwest corner of Califor
nia and Jones streets, was still kepÂ» up
with the necessary retinue of servants
which was evidence to her that Mr. Whit
tell had not abandoned his San Francisco
residence.

AND PENROSE
PASS THE LIE
Sensational End of a De
bate on the Floor of
. the Senate.
Trouble Arose Over a Discussion on

on the Quay Question in
the Upper House.
Â»
WASHINGTON, March 15.â "1 assert

This was the sensational retort made

New Hampshire to a statement just made

were astounded and the auditors In the
galleries quivered with excitement. There
had scarcely been the slightest intimation

under discussion the bill appropriating

ousiness. Pending a motion to that ef

stacles in the way of a vote and Indicated
that Galllnger was one of these Senators.
quietly replied that he desired
to be heard on the question, but had not
had an opportunity to speak.
To his statement Penrose retorted that
the New Hampshire Senator had assured
him he did not expect to speak on the
Quay case.
Instantly Gatlinger was on his feet, and
with evident feeling and with great ve
hemence, replied as quoted in the fore
going.
"I don't know whether I don't speak
the truth." hotly replied Penrose. "or
whether the Senator from New Hamp
shire failed to tell me the truth."
The proposition to tix-a time for a vote
was postponed untU to-morrow, but it was
some time before the excitement subsided.

veloped a decided difference of opinion, as

stitute for the measure a bill to return

NEW HAVEN GIRL
ENDS LONG SLEEP
Strange Seven Weeks' Trance Into Which a
Young Woman Fell After an
Extended Waltz.
NEW HAVEN. March 13.â After lying nearly seven weeks In a trance, caused
by excessive indulgence in dancing, Miss Pauline Fitzgerald has awakened and
askM for food. .
"I have had such a nice dream." she said. "I thought I was waltzing for
hours ar.l hours. I never seemed to tire. But now that I awake I feel tired and
hungry."'
Dance music was recently tried with partial success to awaken Miss Fitzger
ald. The experiment was tried at the sugeestion of the Springside Home, whither
the yo-jr.g woman r.-as conveyed from the home of Mrs. Fred Grant.
When tH<? familiar strain? of a waltz fell upon her ear the girl made spas
modic movements and her lips moved in efforts to articulate. But she soon re
lar.-^d ir.tn Â«tupor.
MiÂ« Fitzgerald's ca^e has attracted the attention of several physicians, who
were present when her cot was moved into the parlor of the Springside Home,
while an attendant experimented with dance music at the piano.
The authorities at the Springsjde Home repeatedly tried, hyponotism, but all
efforts of this kind produced no result.
When Miss Fitzgerald finally awoke she was so weak that her physicians
would allow her to talk but little. To a correspondent she said:
"I can't realize that I have been asleep over six weeks. It seems only like a
lor.g night. I have no recollection of my dreams except that I seemed to be al
ways whirling to the sound of music.
'If you would firmly impress a thing: on
a man's memory tell it to his wife.

ON THE PRESIDIO LINKS.
Qualifying Round of Council's Cup
for Women Will Be Played
To-Day.
A competition- over eighteen holes,
medal play, was held yesterday among
eight caddies of the San Francisco Golf
Club, who had previously qualified by
making the lowest scores over nine holes,
medal play. The prizes offered were sets
of golf clubs, but the contest turned out
unsatisfactory from the lack of scorers to
keep tab on the boys. No award was
made of the prizes, but a competition will
probably be held at some later day.
To-day the qualifying round, over eight
een holes, medal play, of the competition
for the Council's cup, for women, will be
held on the Pres'dto links. All score
cards must be signed by a scorer and
handed In at the clubhouse before sunset
The eight ladies who make the lowest
scores will be entitled to enter the first
round for the cup. which will be played
on Monday, March 13. The semi-finals
will be played Wednesday, the 21st. and
the finals Friday, the 23d.

FANNING'S NEW RECORD.
Calif ornian Makes 175 Straight Kills
at the Traps.
NEAV YORK. March 15.â Jack Fanning,
the crack wing shot of California, created
a newcontinuous match record at the traps
on Madison Square Garden roof to-day,
breaking 175 inanimate targets straight.
This record was accomplished without a
rest.
Although Farming's record .of 173
straight kills has been surpassed, it has
never been accomplished before in a con
tinuous match. The best record of straight
kills is 213. but the record was made" in
different events, that Is. the shooter made
straight kills in several different events,
resting between each event.
Farming's record is exceptionally brilliant
In view of the fact. that It was accom
plished in -the face of a blinding smw
storm. His miss, the one hundred ani
seventy-sixth straight, was comparatively
easy, but was lost through carelessness.
Another phenomenal record was nuns up'
on the rifle range by L. F. Ross of the
Elite Rifle' Club of Brooklyn. Rosa estab
lished a new record of 2429 out of a possi
ble 2500 points. He held the best previous
record of 2425, made at Madison Square
Garden last year.

Knocks Out Eddie Lenny in Six Past
Rounds.
PHILADELPHIA, March 15.â A large
crowd saw Terry McGovern, champion
feather-weight of the world, defeat Eddie
Lenny of Philadelphia at the Industrial
Athletic Club to-night In the second round
of what was to have been a six-round
contest. Lenny was completely out
classed, and in the final round was
knocked down three times in the one min
ute and forty-five, seconds consumed.
Lenny's finish, In the opinion of the fight-
Ing men present', nad the appearance ol
his having quit.
The first knock-down was the only hard
punch of this round, beln? a swinging
right on the local man's jaw. He took the
full ten seconds, and then McGovern
rushed at him with his usual speed and
with a couple -of light body blows sent
him to the floor again. The . local man
again took the full time in coming up.
when after a couple of passes Terry land
ed a light tody blow on Lenny, following
it up with a right on the jaw. Lenny was
then counted out. ,"
The saml-wlnd-up was between Tommy
Feltz of Brooklyn and Jack Landing of
Camden at ICS pounds. It was a spirited
struggle from the start to the end of the
fifth round, when the Brooklyn man
forced Lansing to the edge of the ring
and with a light punch sent Lansing
through the ropes and he fell to the floor.
The fall Jarred Lansing considerably and
he said he was tco weak to continue.
Feltz had clearly the better of the go. his
body blows especially being effective.

A MASSACHUSETTS ABERRATION.
piZjROM several of our Eastern exchanges we learn
I k that a Judge in Massachusetts recently permitted
* a young criminal found guilty of forgery to go
free on condition that he would keep out of Massachu
setts for two years. It appears the Judge regards two
years' banishment from that commonwealth as
equivalent to two years in the State prison, and his
action was based upon that view of the case.
Our Eastern contemporaries, published in States
outside of the :New England group, denounce the ac
tion of the Judge in severe ternis. From the tone of
their remarks we- infer that they look* upon the sen
tence of the Judge as conferring a reward upon the
criminal instead of. a punishment, but their main ob
jection is that Massachusetts is becoming altogether
too thrifty and economical in the dealing with her
criminals. Conceding that to a Boston forger an
exile from the State for two years is really as painful
as two years in the penitentiary, there remains in that
form of punishment the objectionable element that
instead of taking charge of the criminal herself for
that length of time, Massachusetts puts him off upon
some other State.
. It appears California has a particular interest in the
case,' for the Baltimore American, in commenting
upon it, states incidentally as a minor feature of it
that the young criminal's father promised the Judge
to send him to California and keep him there for the
full term of the two years. Possibly the young man
may turn out well and develop here a virtue he did
not possess at home. Such things frequently happen.
All the same, we would prefer to obtain our immi
giants in another way.
International law and formal treaties forbid one
Government from getting rid of its criminal class. by
banishing convicts to exile, in another country. We
have frequently had to complain of European Gov
ernments in that respect, and now we keep a close
watch to see that no nation uses any part of the
United States as a dumping-ground for persons con
victed of crime. It will be a sad day. if one Ameri
can State should have to stand on guard in the same
way against another State. It was quite common, in
deed, at one time, for Eastern communities', to rid
themselves of certain kinds of criminals by permit
ting them to go free on condition they would set out
for Texas or California. The customs of that day
have long. since been outgrown, and it is to be re
gretted a Massachusetts Judge has seen fit to under
take to revive them. Had he sentenced the forger to
banishment to Great Britain or Germany, his conduct
would have been the subject of an international con
troversy at once, and it would seem that there should
be some law to protect California as well as foreign
countries from such exhibitions of Massachusetts
thrift. . ; ' \ :
FASHION HINT FROM PARIS.
ANOTHER BRYAN INVASION.
/"^ALIFORNIA is destined to more'trouble. .We
( have had drought, floods, fires and the San
'^Â¦^ Francisco Board of Health, Phelan's reform
government and the Examiner as an endemic disease
and now it is announced that Bryan impends once
more! I He is to begin in Washington, put his well
worn figures of speech on the Puget Sound market
and then, beginning in Southern California, will go
through the State like a liver regulator.
He selects Southern California at a time when the
tourist crop is harvested and gathered into barns and
the people will have settled down to figure out the ef
fects of a prolonged dry spell. He does' not appear
as a rain-maker. He depends upon an anhydrous
atmosphere and withered fields. Where nature has
foreclosed upon the ordinary fruits and farinacea there
he is with the people, to charge the famine of the fields
to the party in power. The Gulf stream and the Japan
current in their normal position are his enemies. His
allies are the weevil and cut worm, thrips. San Jose
scale, red spider and curled leaf. The Vedalia Aus
tralis he views with alarm and points with pride to the
cushiony cotton scale.
Though the gold standard bill is the law and our 2
per cent bonds are at a premium of 7 per cent and
the money circulation per capita is greater than ever
before, as is the stock of gold, he scents ruin in the air
because our standard of value. is not a silver dollar
worth 50 cents and our bonds are not at a discount
instead of a premium.
Just why Colonel Bryan feels it necessary to quit his
Nebraska fireside in the bracing March weather and
put his gift of prophecy on tap in the cities of the
coast and plains is not explained.
We suspect that the cause may be found in the re
cent cynical and zoological remark of Hon. Sylvester
Pennoyer of Oregon, a veteran in free silver politics,
who says that Bryan is using the same weapon that
served Samson in his little trouble with the Philistines.
Mr. Pennoyer is of the opinion that Samson was the
only artist in the use of that means of overcoming an
enemy and that Bryan plies his jawbone in vain. Bi
this as it may, his visit will greatly refresh the weary
brethren and put life and mettle in the heels of those
who run to politics.
He will diagnose the trust disease and suggest him
self as the only pill which will purge it out of the na
tional constitution. Â¦ After urging on the Spanish war
"because it would be good politics" and securing the
ratification of the purchase of the Philippines when
Republican Senators had it beaten, he will oppose that
imperialism which Senators McLaurin of South Caro
lina, Morgan of Alabama and other leaders of his
party 'declare is the proper caper for this country. His
organ here, the Examiner, which nailed the flag to the
Philippines, and declared that Congress can govern
external possessions without regard to the consti
tution, will lead the claque in favor of his anti-expan
sion oratory and the full band will respond to his
ancient battle cry of 16 to 1.
Meantime sober-minded people, who believe in
principles and not opportunism in politics, will note
that he attacks everything that is and makes no feas
ible suggestion of anything better to take its place.
They will see no remedy for any existing evil in giving
the country over to him and Altgeld, Tillman, Croker,
Fred White, Governor Waite, General Weaver and the
crowd of anarchist agitators whose past records brand
them as incompetent in government and unsafe in
politics. The steady heads and hands of the country
must plan a way out of existing complications and
then carefully work that plan out to a finish. Oppor
tunism never yet furnished good government to any
country, which must be the fruit of the steady and pa
tient application of fixed principles to public issues.
Colonel Bryan admits that the constitution must be
amended to cover an income tax and his anti-trust
legislation. He also adheres to the Chicago platform
attack on the Supreme Court, which requires another
amendment to the constitution to change the method
of choosing that great bench and destroy the life ten
ure of its members. It is plain therefore that Colonel
Bryan's politics is extra-constitutional and that every
policy of importance which he advocates is at war
with our present constitutional system. It Is for our
steady people to say whether they will put into hi?
hands the power to sew patches in the constitution
until it will look like a beggar's gaberlunzie for sale at
Rag Fair.
The wisdom of the fathers who made the consti
tution has been approved by the history of a century
and eleven years of prosperous and successful govern
ment under it. But Colonel Bryan is not pleased with
that great fundamental law and desires to amend it
awry with its purposes. He desires to smash the watch
to make it keep better time. We are persuaded that
the people p-efer to let it stand as great men made it
and not trust its amendment to the clowns and harle
quins who train in Colonel Bryan's perpetual, peri
patetic show. "

upon. the, body of Charles Leslie. Forsyth,

1 Polk street last Monday by a : falling
glass door. The Jury, returned a verdict

HIS LAST LECTURE AT
THE DENTAL COLLEGE
Professor Lengfeld Presented "With a
Dressing Case by the Students
of His Class.
Professor Lengfeld has severed his con
nection with the- Dental College of the
University of California, and yesterday
morning delivered his last lecture to ths
students. During the lecture he was sur
prised when Harry E. Gates, who said
that he had been selected by the other
students to take the- opportunity, to prefer
a number of Â¦ complaints, and after enu
merating them he stepped forward and
presented the professor with a handsome
dressing-case as a mark of appreciation
and esteem. After recovering from his
surprise the professor acknowledged the
present. Â¦. He goes East Ina day or two
and the students all wished him a safe
and pleasant journey.

THE DOMESTIC SERVANT PROBLEM
\A RS. EMMONS BLAINE, daughter of Cyrus
/ \ G. McCormick, inventor of the reaping ma
* * chine, and daughter-in-law of James G." Blaine,
has contributed to the solution of the domestic ser
vant problem an experiment which shows on her part
something of the inventive genius of her father and
something of the wide-reaching diplomacy of her
father-in-law. Her plan is to provide for her house
hold two sets of sen-ants, one to report for duty at 6
a. m. and to leave 3t 2 p. m., the other to begin work
when the first set leaves and continue until 10 p. m.
Thus each set will work but eight hours a day and
will have ample leisure for work, study, recreation, so
cial enjoyment or anything else in the way of occupa
tion they may choose to undertake on their own be
half.
The boldness of. the experiment lies in the fact that
when put into operation the system will leave the
household without servants from 10 p. m. to 6 a. m.
Should there be need for service of any kind in the
house between those hours the members of the family
or their guests will do it or it will remain undone.
Visitors will be requested not to remain after 10
o'clock, gucsts'will be instructed not to call for supper
or hot water or anything else after that hour' and all
babies in the mansion will be restrained from crying
for nurses or for lollypop until the first set of servants
come on in the morning.
It appears in fact that the Emmons Blame house
hold is to be managed something like a factory or a
shop. The work of its manifold activities is to be car
ried on strictly within business hours. Promptly
when the last set of servants has completed the eight
March Seventeenth.

of Cape Colony who sympathize with the Boers are
not treated with sufficient severity by the British au
thorities shows a lack not only of magnanimity, but
of common-sense. It is a sure thing that any harsh

DEMOCRACY AND PORTO RICO.
* IGHTEEN months ago the Examiner an
nounced as good Democracy that: "Should it
â * become necessary to administer outside depend-
L-ncits, it is in the power and right of Congress to
ievise any sort of system that may appl., to the con
htions of such dependencies and this power ii in no
.vise limited by the national constitution, nor does its
implication involve any modification in our domestic
?vstem. All these things are familiar knowledge."
On October 3. 1809, in an editorial on the re
ception of Admiral Dewey in New York, the Ex
aminer's twin, Hearst's New York Journal, made it
plain that we are to govern "outside dependencies."
Under the caption, "Expansion in the Air," the
Journal said in big pica type and double-column form:
"What does it all mean? Why has Admiral Dewey,
who thrashed an enemy from whom the country was
never in danger, been welcomed with transports of
enthusiasm that were never evoked by the older heroes
who saved the nation in its struggles for existence?
"What the welcome really means, aside from appre
ciation of the charm of Dewey's personality, is that
the vast mass of Americans approve with all their
fouls the consequences of his victory. As for the
victory itself, as a mere feat of arms, that plays a
minor part in the rejoicings. Suppose, for instance,
that the fleet had been ordered to sail home imme
diately after the battle, as some thought it should have
been, and that the Philippines had been left to Spain,
does anybody suppose that Dewey would have had the
place in our national pantheon that he occupies now?
"Or suppose the masses held Atkinson's opinions
about expansion, is it likely that they would have
worked themselves up to a frenzy of enthusiasm over
Dewey? The reception of the admiral's name in
Atkinsonian meetings is sufficient answer to the ques
tion. The manifest truth is that the people who
cheered for Dewey were believers in expansion, as he
b himself. Compare the seven miles of packed and
howling humanity on Saturday with a meeting in a
third-story hall and you will have the measure of the
expansion and anti-expansion sentiment among the
American people."
The Journal then quotes ex-Democratic Governor
Fattison of Pennsylvania in favor of expansion and
s::ys: "He was twice elected Governor of Pennsyl
vania, a State whose Republican majorities are usu
ally counted in six figures. He is experienced in read
ing the signs of popular feeling, and when he says
that in his recent travels in the West he lias seen no
sign of a backward movement anywhere, and asks
v hether the Dewey outpouring in New York indicated
r:iy abatement in national enthusiasm, Democrats will
do well to give thoughtful attention to his- observa
tions." Remember, this was last October. The
Philippine war was on then, as it is now. There is no
change anywhere in the expansion situation which
the Hearst papers regarded with such approval. But
tlie moment their idea of government unlimited by
the constitution is applied to our "outside de
pendencies" they shriek "infamy" and denounce
tlie Porto Rico tariff because it is unconstitutional!
Such opposition is unworthy of consideration. It is
below contempt. Those two papers demand that we
must have these outside dependencies and must gov
ern them outside the constitution and then denounce
our possession of them and our first attempt at such
government!
Suppose the party they represent get into power,
what policy will it enforce? Will it extend the con
stitution to these dependencies and make equal citi
zens of their eighteen millions of people? Or will it
keep the constitution at home and govern them as it
pleases and how?
Or will it drop these outside dependencies, for the
ownership of which it howled? The people are not to
be fooled. They demand of a political opposition
something more than opposition as to grave issues.
They want a plain declaration of policy. Republican
anti-expansionsists prefer their own party and th-;
prospect of controlling it to a right solution to help
ing into power a mob that can only destroy and has
no ability to construct

HUNTINGTON'S INSURANCE SCHEME CONDEMNED.
Nlles Herald.
IN lies xieiiin.l. tl -_ -
One of the most outrageous infringements upon a man's rights is the reiier
department" of the Southern Pacific Company, which has recently been promul
gated. It forces every employe, regardless of his position or desires, to pay a nign
premium to the corporation for sick and death benefits, and to do so whether ne
has other protection or not. Â¦ , _â-_* lv n
One of the very strongest objections is the fact that the station as" 1 *;?â¢"
is as free from liability to accident as an editor, shall pay the same P re "" u â¢_,^
a switchman, who takes his life into his hands every hour of the day. Tfteomy
classification of risks is the salary, each man being bled for a certain per cent oi
Another strong objection is the clause exempting the company from damage
suits or forfeiture of all claims for insurance. .
This is a free country and no corporation should be allowed to cinch its em
ployes as the Southern Pacific Company is making an effort to do. Its hospital
system is now robbing the men of thousands of dollars per year. The men
should be protected by law, as they cannot do anything themselves at the riaK
of losing their positions. . . . .
If pressed, the men will no doubt strike, and then the State will have to again
go through the scenes of IS!M, but every fair-minded man should stand witn.tne
railroad men and give them every assistance in his power in their fight for per
sonal liberty as to how and where they shall take their Insurance.
The press of the State should take up their battle and see to it that the next
Legislature passes laws which will prevent this highway robbery and force all
corporations to give a public statement of the receipts and disbursements every
six months of all funds collected from their employes. 'v* v -
â¢ â¢ â¢
Benicia New Era.
The Insurance scheme of the Southern Pacific does not seem to find much favor
with their employes. The opposition is widespread and the explanations so far
made by Mr. Kruttschnltt and the heads of the different departments have not
been satisfactory to the employes. The majority of the men are Insured In
some mutual insurance organization, in which they receive all the Insurance
they are in need of at a far less cost than C. P. Huntington's scheme. The
burden imposed is too heavy on their Income; besides the other features of thi3
plan will bring a state of unrest that is bound to lead to serious consequences.
The fraternal part of Mr. Huntington does not seem to appeal to his employes
and we can hardly blame them, as there seems- to be some sinister design which
will probably creep out later. If all the railroad organizations on this coast will
stand together and make Mr. Huntington understand that they do not propose to
be coerced, his pet measure will be abandoned.
San Benlto Advance.
The scheme of the Southern Pacific Company to inaugurate an insurance
and relief department among their host of employes has met with the most
serious opposition. While the general scheme of relief is good, there are features
connected therewith which are unfair, and which if not withdrawn will lead to
serious trouble and discontent. Unless the railroad officials who are Introduc
ing the relief scheme can make a better showing than they have up to date,
it will never become popular with the railroad employes. And in their resistance
to what is put forward as philanthropy, they will have the hearty indorsement" of
the intelligent traveling public.

THE CLIMAX OF FAKE FIGHTING
HAD. any further evidence been required to con
vince the public that the so-called sport of prize
fighting has in this' city become nothing more
than a fake scheme for fleecing those who are foolish
enough to bet on the contests, it would have been fur
nished by the match between Stelzner and Butler un
der the management of the National Athletic Club at
Woodward's Pavilion on. Wednesday evening. The
thing was so clearly a prearranged scheme to defraud
bettors that the referee refused to stand for it, and
even the club managers became ashamed. The ref
eree declared all bets off as soon as the men entered
the ring, and the managers told them after the fight
that it was so palpably a fake they would receive noth
ing for their exhibition.
While that is perhaps the "rawest" game of the
kind ever put up in San Francisco, it does not stand
alone. Again and again the deluded patrons of the ring
and of pugilism have been treated to similar exhibitions
and have lost their money by betting upon matches
which were fixed beforehand. They were in fact on
Wednesday evening more fortunate than usual, for
by the action of the referee they were saved from the
loss of money bet upon the match and are victims only
to the extent of the amounts paid out to enter the
pavilion and see the fake.
Such a fraud, coming on top of the many that pre
ceded it, caps the climax and completes the exposure
of the whole rotten system of public prize-fighting.
It makes clear the need of the enactment of just such
an ordinance as that submitted to the Supervisors by
the irate Mr. Tobin on Monday. In fact it seems the
promoters of the fake had come to the conclusion the
Tobin ordinance will be adopted and hastily arranged
the Stelzner and Butler programme to make a clean
up before it goes into effect.
Having had this much of an exposition of the tricks
of the trade it is to be hoped the betting public will be
sufficiently intelligent to profit by it and let that form
of gambling alone. 1 The" pugs should.be forced to go
East or go to work. There should be no further tol
eration for them in San Francisco any more than for
the Eastern contingent of track, gamblers who have
been trying to; obtain the 'privilege of operating at
Ingleside. Enough is as good as ; a feast at all times
and in this : particular business there has now been
spread forth enough rottenness to satisfy buzzards.
THE SAN ...FBAXCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, "MARCH 16. 1900.
hours of its allotted time for duty the household will
close up like a California poppy at sunset and not open
its radiant life again until the next sunrise.
â¢There is much in the project to be commended.
Every class of workers is entitled to some respite from
work, to some time of leisure which they can call their
own. One of the chief reasons why American girls
dislike domestic service is the continuous .round of
work it imposes.- When' the factory closes for the
day the factory girl's work for that day is done and
thereafter until next morning she is her own mistress.
That degree of liberty" is 'highly prized. and if, it can
be provided for employes in domestic service there can
be no question but what it will go Â¦' far; toward making
that form of work more attractive than it is.
Whether or not the improvement can be effected on
a basis sufficiently economical to render it. practicable
remains to be seen. Mrs. Eramons Blame will prob
ably find that she needs three sets of servants instead
of two to carry out the work of her household on the
eight-hour plan. She is rich and can afford it. Her
neighbors who are not rich will watch her experi
ment with an attention due to something more than
feminine curiosity. They will wish to see how they
can adopt the system without diminishing their mil
liners' bills or ruining their husbands.

VIâ EXPOSING THE PLATE.
When we are ready to withdraw t!ic
slide so as to expose the plate, that is. to
allow the picture as we have just seen It
upon the ground glass to be thrown ;n the
same way upon the face of the dry plate
in our holder and the image of the picture?
to be impregnated upon the plate. w. e
must again caution against overexposure.
We shall learn later on of exposure
meters for mechanically judging of ex
posure. At present we shall endeavor to
get as correct an exposure as possible by
our own judgment, which after all s the
best exposure meter. This judging of cor
rect exposure is a part of photograoliy
which should receive constant study. Ex
pert knowledge Is secured only through
close observation and long experience.
Suppose that we have a medium plate
in the holder and that our view Is made
up of a clump of trees, a brook, a few
large stones and an old log bridge. It is
a fairly bright day and the sun is shlntiiy.
After studying the time of exposure by
looking first at the view and then at IUJ
image on the ground glass, as already de
scribed, we conclude that with the stop or
diaphragm that we have used to get our
picture at its best five seconds is
about the correct time. Our lens being
closed, either by placing the cap. on th<
projecting hood of the lens or by .closing
the shutter, we take. hold of the slide wltl.
the fingers of the right hand, placing th.
left upon the top of the camera over tro
slideholder in order to steady the camera,
and draw out the slide completely. V-c
must be very careful to draw it over per
fectly straight, for the reason that the end
of the holder through which the slHe is
drawn is protected by a spring or cushion
of black velvet extending the width or the
sliue. If we draw out the slide with a
downward motion the upper end leaves
the holder first, while the lower end. still
In the slide, holds the cushion from pei
forming its function, which is to close up
the slot when the elide is out and thus
prevent the rays of light from pas. rg
through to the plate, which means tlia..
our plate will be fogged, or light struck.
"It ls'then of great importance that we
draw our slide out gently., yet auickh.
and draw it straight. After drawing the
slide it is well to acquire the habit of
throwing the side of tte focuslnff ciotn
over the side of the camera from % h eh
the slide has been withdrawn, thus doully
Detecting the end of our holder from tna
Fff Especially should this be done in
outdoor work. , f ...
Now. to count our seconds and to count
them as seconds: it is remarkaMe hovk
differently as to time this may be "onf.
of course w e may use a watch and get the
time exact but it is claimed, and with
trath. by good camerists that to expose
mechanically is to IoÂ»Â» the soul of the
nirtnre for when once one Is ma3ter
of timing a picture under different phases
tnere 3 an nner feeling of conception of
the depth of the picture that guides the
hand and eye in making exposures. Be
this as it may. it is true that nine people
out of ten expose by rule of eye or count.
V havlfound that by a very little prac
tice almost correct time may be gUen in
seconds by countingâ one secondâ two sec
onds-three seconds distinctly and in the
usual speed of the voice in talking. Â¦To
try to count by saying one. two. three,
will rarely land the beginner within half
Of the time he expects. Begin counting
as the shutter, is opened or the lens un
capped and close the lens at the end of
the last "second." Don't be tempted to
give "just a little more time.' for more
plates are spoiled by the "little more time"
than by almost any other means '
The exposure being made the slide is re
placed, and here we must remember to
place the end of the slide exactly straight
in the opening, and keep it straight, for to
introduce one corner first would ruin our
nlate Just as we have seen above. In re
placing the slide be sure to have the dark
side of the slide on the outside, as this
will show that the plate has been exposed
and will keep us from making the quite
common mistake of exposing the same
P Right here and now is the time for us to
begin a system of "method," by which we
mean' labeling each exposure and Jotting
down a quick memorandum of the view
taken, the plate used, the stop used and
the time or exposure: also whether the
day is dull, medium or light. This tabu
lating will mean the saving of a great
many plates and a deal of wear and tear
on our temper when we come to develop
our pictures, not only on this initiative
; trip but in all our future work. There
is nothing so exasperating as to return
home from a photographing jaunt with
a half dozen choice views, taken and timed
under a half dozen different conditions of
light and speed, only to forget in the dark
room "which are which." Let us decide
now that we shall always carry a mem
orandum book on our trip and jot down
particulars somewhat as follows:
PLATE NO. 1.
View, landscape, dark foliage; brook, log
brldze In foresround.
Lensâ Goers, 4x3. * n
Plate â Cramer, "Banner.
Timeâ Five seconds.
Llirlitâ Bright. 2 p. m.
Result.....
And so on with each exposure. Of course
each plate-holder must .be numbered so
that No. 1 plate corresponds with No. 1
"memo." By keeping a careful record of
each exposure in this manner we soon
learn by comparison and by early mis
takes the proper exposure to give under
any given .circumstances.
We have cautioned against overexpos
ure for the reason that the almost uni
versal failing of the beginner Is to over
expose, but a little overexposure Is more
easily handled In development than is un
