FIGHTING TO SAVE
CAPE NOME'S TRADE
Pacific Coast Merchants Endeavor to
Block the Scheme of Canadian
Shipowners at Washington.
For the past few weeks the Chamber of
Commerce and the Pacific Coast repre
sentatives at Washington have been at
work to prevent the authorities at the na
tional capital acceding to the wishes of
British Columbia shippers to have Nome
City a sub-port of entry. It was learned
here that shipowners whose vessels are
flying, the flag of her Majesty have been
advertising, to carry freight and pas&en
gers direct to Nome as soon as the weath
er would permit. No time was lost in urn-
Ing Western Representatives to block the
game, so as to give the owners of Amer
ican coasting vessels the business, which
rightfully belongs to them, vlf Nome is
not made a sub-port of entry British ship,
pers will have to land at Dutch Harbor
or St. Michael iind, send their passengers
and cargoes to the new gold flleds by
American vessels. Senator Perkins and
others have presented the Â¦ matter to the
Treasury Department. - receiving the as
surance that Nome "would be held for
American shippers only. - :
Recently, the Republican Represcnta
tives learned that the Canadian shippers
were working to influence the' Treasury
Department to reconsider, the. mattfr, so
as to allow their ships the same opportu
nities as those owned in the United States.
The claim of the Canadian shippers Is that
there are not enough American vessels to
handle the freight.
Captain Nelson of the Chamber of Com
merce yesterday sent the following dis
patch to Secretary of the Treasury GaÂ»e:
r 'Our merchants and shipowners urgently
request you not to make Nome City a sub
port of entry. They are abundantly tble
to furnish transportation' and supplied for
all^possible wants." Â¦' Â¦ Â¦ .

Teeth Without Plates F.OO
when there Is something to hold back, but
It is impossible to get one when there is
notnlng to bring* up. for if tne exposure
is too Khort then tnere is not sufficient on
the plute to make a picture, and no
amount oi developing will bring out what
is not there.
We shall find that a number of factors
enter into the question of l:ght. The time
of the year, the time of day and tne con
dition of tne atmosphere, the clouda la
the sky, etc., ait or any one of tn<sm may
make decided differences In Â»ne quantity
und quality of the light.
Subjects that are of an open character
and have no masses of deep shadow near
the camera require snurt exposures,
whereas suDJects with massta oi sr.ado
In the foreground require long exposures.
Where a large portion of the subject is
under trees, as in the case of forest glades
or wooded ravines, very long exposures
may be required, because the greater port
of the light has been filtered through the
green leaves overhead, and this holds
good even though the camera itself Is not
under the trees. The color of the objects
also influences the time of exposure; a
yellow. stone building requires longer ex
posure than one of white stone, and a
red brick building will require still longer.
Interiors of buildings always require
longer exposures than ordinary outdoor
subjects, but they show such great differ
ences, according to the size and posi
tion of the windows and the color of the
glass, that the time required varies from
less than a minute to three or four hours,
or even more. Subjects including a large
Â¦proportion of sea and sky require, as a
rule, very short exposures. The time of
exposure In the case of portraits varies
very much, and depends on the conditions
of lighting and also on the character of
the effect desired.
The light is most active and the ex
posures required an vnortesfl tn June, and
on a bright day the activity of the light
remains practically the same between 9
a. m. and 3 p. m., and It is equally active
in May and July from ID a. m. to 2 p. m.
In spring and autumn longer exposures
are required, and In winter the time must
be from three to ten times as long as in
June, according to the hour of the day.
The light is least active in December, and
the exposure required becomes shorter
and shorter as we approach June, and
afterward becomes longer and longer until
the maximum is again reached in Decem
ber. The light increases In brightness
from early morning until midday, when
it attains a maximum, and then falls off
again until sunset, the falling off being
considerably more rapid in winter than
In summer. In very early morning and
toward sunset the exposures are always
long, especially if the light la at all yel
low.
When the sun is shining and there are
white clouds in the sky opposite the sun
the exposures needed are shorter. ot"her
things being equal, than when the sky is
perfectly cloudless. A cloud actually In
front of the sun of course lengthens the
necessary exposure very considerably, and
the same Is true in a smaller degree of
light cloudy that do 'not completely ob
scure the sun. Rain clears the atmos
phere of dust, etc., and, other things be
ing equal, less exposure is required after
a shower.
It will be clear that the estimation of
correct exposure requires Judgment and
experience. The conditions are never con
stant and hence numerical tables are only
very approximate, although they afford
some assistance at the beginning. Not
much help can be obtained from actlno
meters because they only measure the
intensity of the light where the photo
graphor is standing, while what we ought
to measure is the Intensity of the light
reflected from the different parta of the
subject. Now and again, and especially
when dealing with interiors, they will tell
us that the light is much yellower and
less active than we should otherwise have
believed.
The photographer must, learn to rely
upon his own judgment, guided by the
nature of the subject, with a special re
gard to the proportion and depth of the
shadows; the apparent brightness of the
image on the ground glass, the time of
year, the time of day and the character
of the light, especially with regard to the
presence of clouds â even light ones â be
tween the sun and earth. Brightness to
the eye is not necessarily a measure of
activity on the photographic plate, and.
aa already pointed out, any yellowness â
even though slightâ ln the light length
ens considerably the time of exposure re
quired. Sufficient exposure should always
be given for the dark parts of the sub
ject; the brightly Illuminated parts will
take care of themselves. The exposure
given will also depend on the ' character
of the results required: very short expo
sures increase the contrast and long ex
posures reduce the contrast. If, there
fore, you consider that the contrast of
light and shade in the subject is too
strong for pictorial effect, give a longer
exposure, and vice versa. For example, a
view including whitewashed cottages or
other brilliantly lighted objects, together
with foliage, will require a comparatively
long exposure if harsh contrasts in tbe
negative are to be avoided.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, Â» FRIDAY, MARCH 16, 1900.
Rival Bivalve Diggers Engage in a Quarrel Which Is Settled in Court.
RUBBER- NECK clams, properly prepared, are
morsels of food that gourmands are said to rave
over. Michael Connell and James Connell, brother
clam-digKers, are not gourmands. In fact, if asked
what gourmands were, they would probably shake
their heads and say that they did not inhabit the mud
flats that surround the bay shore at the foot of Thirty
sixth street. The Connells are conversant with big neck
and little neck clams and the' best time and place to
gather them. They are also experts on landing "rubber
neck" clams. The oldest descendant of the Connell tribe
has beenengagedin this lucrative pastime for such a long
period that when a festive clam stretches its neck out of
the mud to gaze at its surroundings Michael snips him be
fore the bivalve can escape.
The fame of the. Connells as clam-dlggers is not confined
to the district In which they reside. When both appeared
before Judge Cabaniss yesterday to answer to charges of
battery against Fred de Crolx, a rival clam-gatherer, the
bailiff of the court whispered to the Judge that they were
"Its," with capital Is.
Judge Cabaniss did not lose his presence of mind, but
greeted the illustrious clam-diggers with proper feeling.
He invited the defendants and their relentless prosecutors
to chairs and then began an investigation that will go down
In Police Court annals as the "clammiest" Investigation
ever held. Mr. de Crblx, who parts his name In the middle,
told a sad story, of prosecution and suffering. He has a
"clam bed" which adjoins that of the Connells. The clams
on his side of the line have imbibed the bitter feeling, that
exists between the rivals' arid they do not visit each other.
The dividing line between the clam beds shifts with, each
changing tide and this results in warm arguments and
oftentimes blows. Â¦
A few days ago De Crolx crossed the dividing line to
remonstrate with 'the- Connells. He was treated unnelgh
borlyâin fact, he was thrown Into the ooze that lies at the
foot of the Connell mansion, and as he sank he heard Pat
Malacky and the Connells Veil derisively at his plight.
When . he succeeded' in reaching dry land and had shaken
the clams out of his boots he hied himself to the Police
Court and swore to complaints against both men and Pat
Malacky for giving him a mud bath and the "ha ha."
Judge Cabannls fourd the Connells guilty as charged and
will sentence them to-d?.y: The Connells will to-day prob
ably swear out a warrant for De Crolx's arrest for stealing
their clams.
SPRING EXCURSION
IS OFF THIS YEAR
The promoters of the Manufacturers'
and Wholesalers' League of San Francisco
have decided to abandon the spring ex
cursion this yeaf. Excursions were 'ar
ranged to bring purchasers from all parts
of the continent west of the Mississippi.
The inducement was that the outside
merchants would be allowed a rebate of
one-half their iailroad fare provided they
bought their stock from members of the
league.
Large numbers took advantage of the
offer, but the league found that the out
siders made purchases only in certnin
lines, and that while the wholesalers and
manufacturers of these articles did tvell
the other subscribing members of the
league sold but little and had to stand
thPlr proportion of tho fxivn>p.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
A trough at low pressure extends from Arl
rona through California into Oregon. The pres
ture hae fallen steadily over the northern half
cf the Faclti<- slope. There are as yet no ln
oiratlons of the approach of a storm, but con
ditions are such that should a storm develop
oo the North Pacific it would probably move
rapidly southward along the coast.
The temperature has remained nearly sta
tionary weft of the Rocky Mountains, except
at AÂ«t?rla, where there has been a fall of 24
cejrrefs in the past twenty-four hours.
No rain has fallen on the Pacific Coast.

NEW TORJC, March 15.â Business was gen

THE STOCK MARKET.
* : *
On the morning session of the Bond Ex
change the only change worthy of note was a
decline in Giant Powder to $S4. Business was
quiet. ...-.'-
In the afternoon Giant Powder declined to
$S1 and Market-Street Railroad advanced to
The oil stocks were unchanged.
The California Fruit Canneis' Association
has declared the second dividend of 60 cents
per share, payable on the 20th.
The Potosl Mining Company has levied an
assessment of 15 cents per share, delinquent
The Confidence Mining Company has levied
an assessment of 20 cents per share, delinquent
The Horseshoe Bar Consolidated Mining Com
pany of Placer and El Dorado counties has
levied an assessment of 2 cents per share, de
linquent April 14.

CHICAGO, March 15.-The early trading in
wheat was not marked by any degree of ac
tivity or any marked tone. The opening for
May was at 66H@66%c. a decline of . VÂ»Â®%c
from yesterday, the slight loss being" due In
part to disappointment over the reluctant're
sponse of the Liverpool market to yesterday's
advance here. The loss was recovered almost
Immediately. May advancing to 66 T /Â»c under
covering by local shorts, but before 11 o'clock
weakness again developed and kept prices on
the down grade for nearly an hour. During this
part of the session the foreign demand for
American wheat was reported poor, while ex
pectations of heavy Argentine wheat shipments
to-morrow, a feature which has knocked good
fractions off of the price of wheat for three
successive Fridays, resulted In considerable
short selling, the theory being that to-mor
row's opening would show a decline. About
noon, however, the market turned quite strong
on rumors of crop damage and the price which
had In the meantime fallen off to 66 t 4<g6ii'HsC.
advanced to 67c. But this was thÂ» price that
heavy holders had apparently been waiting for
and offerings became so heavy that VÂ«c was
knocked off May figures In a hurry. At 6C?ic
a good demand existed and the market held
eteady at around that figure for some time,
re|>orts of a good demand for export at out
side shipping points having considerable In
fluence. Shortly before the close realizing
sales and uneasiness over the weakness shown
by corn caused another decline and the tone
during the last few minutes was one of weak
ness. May eased off ito 66% c and closed at
6â¬%c.
Corn was only moderately active. Early In
the session there were Indications ' that in
fluential holders were disposing of their May
and buying July, and this resulted in con
siderable liquidation. Heavy clearances had a
steadying Influence later, sellers turning buy
ers, but toward the close the market again
developed a tendency to decline and the 'close
was rather easy. May closed H- lower at
Trading In oats was almost without feature
and was on a small scale all day. May closed
at 23% c.
Provisions were moderately active and rather
Irregular within a small range. Packers were
fair sellers, but pit traders showed a dispo
sition to cover shorts, which, with some com
mission house buying, kept the market fairly
steady most of the session. May pork closed
5c lower. May lard a shade lower and May
ribs unchanged.

Hongk'njr ManrChlna and Japan IMar. 23Veterans Want Protection.
A meetrnK of the Veteran's Civil War
Association was held last evening. The
principal matter discussed was the form
ing of a State organization. After some
debate the following resolution was
adopted:
Whereas. W* find In all Federal. State and
municipal departments undue discrimination
practiced against veterans of the Civil War.
and whereas, the above-named departments
have adopted a civil service system detrimental
to the interact of \-eterans. in&smucfa as the
beÂ»t school days of the veterans were epent In
<3Â»fenÂ«Â« of their country, therefore be It
Renolved, That a State organization be
formed for the mutual protection of veterans
on! eons cf veterans ia order to prevent any
further discrimination.
BROWNIES IN
FAIRYLAND
"The Brownies in Fairyland" made their
bow to a crowded house at the California
Theater yesterday afternoon and con
vinced the audience that the town is chock
full of precocious youngsters, and that
every one of them is either a "brownie"
or a "fairy." â¢
There is nothing small about the Brown
ies, excepting, of course, the performers.
Everything else Is Immense. The scenery
and costuming are elaborate, and the
vocal gems and dainty dances with which
the meager story is plentifully stuffed Is
such as would do honor to a production
of talented professionals.
Â¦ To single out any of the performers for
special mention would be not only unfair,
but Impossible. There were no "better"
actors; all were "best." Tiny tots who
seemed to be taking yesterday for the first
time steps without the kindly aid of a.
chair broke into the swaying motion of
the Cakewalk.
The "fairies," for once In the history of
the stage, were joung, and the "brownies"
happy and brave, with just the right sort
of ppindle-shanked legs. Last evening
again the "fairies" disported themselves
in sylvan groves and the "brownies" van
quished their formidable foe. The same
performance will, go on again this after
noon and evening, with a double repetition
The list of the talented little ones and
the characters they represent are as fol
lows: .

SUMMING UP OF
HOLMES' CONCERTS
THE symphony season Is at an end.
The final concert of the series took
place yesterday afternoon at the
Grand Opera-house, and Mr. Henry
Holmes laid down his baton with
out, it must be said, having wielded it in
any case with the magic of the master.
I know nothing of the internecine war
fare that raged in the camp of our sym
phony, cult at the beginning of the present
season, and from which Mr. Holmos
sprang as a full-fledged and accredited
leader of symphony, in my ignorance of
the causes that gave rise to undignified
display of petty jealousies and recrimina
tion I am ready Â¦to suspect that Mr.
Holmes was forced into a usurpation of
the post of conductor by some over-zeal
ous and undiscrimlnating hero-worshipers
and equally ready to doubt that he him
self sought a position he was so little cap
able of rilling.
In offering an estimate of the work per
formed during the season I have avoided
comparison as a measure of merit, yet the
standard by which we judge must be in
the' mind it it bo not pronounced.
The contrasting of Mr. Holmes and Mr.
Scheel has been, of bourse, inevitable and
there can be but one conclusion drawn.
\Ve have had one musician, who, as a
leader of orchestra, was dowered with a
superb mastership, beside which the in
aptitude of his successor is almost pitiful,
ilr. Fritz Scheel was that man. He was
not granted the pecuniary support that
has stood in good stead of the present
season and he did not have at his dis
posal the admirable array of instrumen
talists that has worked with Mr. Holmes,
but by sheer force of his enormous genius
(a strong term, I admit, but a just one)
he gave a value to whatever he touched
well nigh unsurpassable.
A review of the season would be but a
catalogue of Inefficient interpretations.
Tschaikowsky. Schubert, Schumann, Bee
thoven and Mendelssohn have been suc
cessively butchered, and all of merit that
there has been in the concerts is due to
Mr. Marquart, a not too talented musi
cian, and the worthy workers of the rank
and ille. I believe Mr. Holmes to have
attainments of no ordinary character; he
is equipped with a superior technical
knowledge, but the essential attributes of
the maestro are not his.
Against the formalism and perverted
authority from which he is Inseparable,
there is a want of spirit, passion, tem
perament that renders his grasp of the
bigger things in music so variously inef
fective. He is wanting also in rhythm
sense and the judicious direction of tempo
and he. has at times conducted numbers
with which he was evidently Ignorant of
the construction, much less the purpose
and possibilities.
Yesterday's concert began with Schu
mann's "Manfred," which was very well
executed by the band, particularly in the
finale. The symphony was Mendelssohn's
"Scotch," wherein the orchestra again ac
quitted itself commendably. The ada
gio, thanks to Mr. Holmes, was -posi
tively racy and in the last movement, al
legro guerriero, the secure workmanship
of the muijlrlans hardly availed against
the utter kosence of vitality and spirit
essential to Its proper rendering.
Not only did Mr. Holmes fail to give
force and color to the movement, but he
conducted it with a more than usual
looseness. He indicated his accents In the
most haphazard fashion and frequently
lost the pace and place altogether. The
Tschaikowsky "Scherzo," which is quite
beyond his ken, was Interesting In itself
and the band played It acceptably if with
out sensational effect. The "William
Tell" overture followed, and thus ended
the inglorious symphony season of 1900.

organized this morning and the following
directors elected: R, E. Jack, A. W.
Steinhart. H. F. Osgood, John Whicher

reopens.
THIRTEEN GRAVES STOP
RAILWAY CONSTRUCTION
Unprecedented Obstacle Encountered
on the Northern Pacific Ex
tension.
Special Dispatch, to The Call.
BOISE/ Idaho, March 15.â The railroad
construction forces have, encountered an
unprecedented obstacle on the Northern
Pacific extension from Stuart to the
mouth of Cottonwood Creek. Directly In
the lino of location, from which there can
be no deviation owing to the canyon
walls, are thirteen graves in ' a row.
graves of Indian children whose fathers
and mothers live in the Kamalh Valley.
Just the width of thirteen graves is the
land needed by the railroad. The prog
ress of the railroad ia not delayed by
force qf arms, but by the awe Inspired
by the universal sentiment of grief for
the dead.
The engineers prepared to remove the
dead from their resting places. The boxes
wore brought and unloaded at the graves.
Then came a pathetic scene that stopped
every spade. From the hearts of mothers
burst forth nature's language of grief. It
was an appeal that no man from official
to shoveler could resist. The boxes that
wpre brought to hold the dead lie there
deserted as though a curse had fallen on
the spot.
Cyrus Beede, the Government .commis
sioner, who has settled so many disputes
with the Indians, is appealed to. He has
a new sentiment to combat. No price
will influence these disconsolate mothers.
No court will condemn a right of way
through a city of the dead. The Indians
and the whites, however, have faith In
Beede, but he says this Is the most deli
cate commission ever imposed on him. ln
his thirty years in the Indian service.
WIDOW BOBBED.
House of Mrs. Longmire at Willows
Entered and Money Taken.
Special Dispatch to The Call
WILLOWS, March 13.â Some person as
yet unknown stealthily entered the home
of Mrs. Emma Longmire, a widow, early
this morning and robbed her of $370. The;
burglar entered the building by cutting a
hole through a screen on the window and
crawling over the foot of the bed in
which Mrs-. Longmlra was lying. Having
gained access to the room the thief en
tered a closet, which was within two feet
of the head of the bed, took a small toy
bank from the shelf within, stepped back
Into the bedroom and proceeded to coolly
remove the contents of the bank, which
amounted to $390.
He would have escaped without notice
had he not dropped a $20 gold piece on
the floor. The noise of the dropping coin
aroused Mrs. Longmire and she uttered a
cry frightening the robber, who beat a
hasty retreat through a back door, tak
ing the remainder of the money, $370.
The officers were quickly notified of the
theft and are now working on the case.
They have been , unable to obtain a clue
but are determined to thoroughly investi
gate the affair.
Mrs. Lonemire has been sick for the
past two months, and is yet quite nervous,
consequently she cannot give an intelli
gent description of the burglar. She is
not in very good circumstances and the
loss falls heavily upon her. It is thought
whoever committed the crime was well
acquainted with the house and the place
where the money was kept. ' :Â¦;

FRATERNITY LIFE AT BERKELEY.
SNOW IN WASHINGTON.
Record Unprecedented for the Season
of the Year.. !
WASHINGTON, March 15.â About eight
inches of snow fell hereto-day and to
night there is a driving hail, with pros
pects of intermittent hail, snow or sleet
until some time to-morrow. Much of the
early snow melted as it fell. The record
is probably unprecedented for. this locality
for this season of the year. Reports re
ceived at the Weather Bureau show that
the storm has prevailed over a great ex
tent of the territory east of the Mississip
pi River, beginning with a light fall in
Northern Louisiana. Quite a heavy fall
of snow occurred in New York. The storm
has reached the borders of; New England
and promises to extend through that re
gion. Colder : weather is following ; the
storm and the Indications point to the
temperature falling near the freezing
point to-night or to-morrow throughout a
large area of the South.

TO THE SEAT OF WAR
IN THE PHILIPPINES
Meade's Passengers Will Include
Hospital Corps, Signal Men,
Nurses and Casuals.
When the Meade sails to-morrow she
will take away all the casuals now re
maining at the Presidio, about thirty in
all, besides some of the convalescent reg
ulars who are fit to return to duty in the
islands. They will all be under command
of First Lieutenant Louis R. Burgess,
Fifth Artillery, who has been home on
sick leave. Lieutenant Burgess was
wounded in the foot during one of the
skirmishes and was sent home early last
January. He is now ready to return to
duty.
The three female nurses who will go
on the Meade are Julia Hinkle. Ellen M.
Baker and Alice M. Boynon. They have
been on duty at the General Hospital for
some time.
The Signal Corps will be represented on
the Meade by a detachment of one first
class private and three second-class pri
vates from Angel Island. Over seventy
men from the Hospital Corps will com
plete the transport's passenger list.
William Kelly, civil service clerk, who
returned from the Philippines some
months ago and has been on duty at de
partment headquarters since then, has
been ordered to proceed to San Antonio,
Texas, there to report for duty to the
commanding general of the department of
Texas.
The presentation of the gun to be given
to the city by General Shafter will take
place next Wednesday at half past 12
o'clock. Two troops of cavalry will lend
color and dignity to the occasion, and the
Native Sons have promised some special
ceremonies of their own. General Shafter
In person will present the gun and Mayor
Phelan will accept it on behalf of the
city. ,

WALSH MUST SUPPORT
HIS TWO CHILDREN
Judge Dunne Refuses to Commit
Youngsters Charged With
Crime to Whittier.
InEtead of being sent to prison to pass
their minority among incorrigibles and
criminals, Edward and Willie Walsh,
youngsters of 14 and 16 years respectively,
will. In all probability, be handed back to
the care of their father and he will leave
the court with them after receiving due
instruction as to the manner in which
he should care for and educate his chil
dren. The youngsters were up before
Judge Dunne yesterday on charges of rob
bery. The Information against them was
signed by Nora Coughlin, their grand
mother, and she charged that In the early
part of January last they robbed her of
$13. After reading the information Judge
Dunne requested the lad's father to step
to the bar.
â¢'How long nave these boys been in
Jail?" asked the court, addressing Walsh.
"About two months," answered the
father.
"Such a condition of affairs Is without
precedent," continued the court, "and
such an experience is enough to ruin them
for life."
Walsh admitted that he was aware of
the evils Â©af prison life, but crept behind
the excuse that he could not prevent the
lower court from holding them for trial.
As it was paid that the children were
held at the father's request, who hoped
to send them to Whittlcr Reform School,
Judge Dunne continued the case for a
week. In the meantime, he said, he would
investigate the matter, and at the end of
the week would probably find that - the
children should be supported and educated
by their father, and not be made a bur
den to the State or thrown into prison
to associate with hardened criminals dur
ing their minority.
MUNICIPAL OWNERSHIP OF
TELEPHONES FAVORED
Supervisors' Public Utilities Commit
tee Considers Projects to Be
Submitted to the People.
The Supervisors' Committee on Public
Utilities met last night in the chambers
of the board for the purpose of getting in
formation and suggestions regarding the
acquisition of public utilities by the mu
nicipality. The investigation was directed
toward deciding on tho selection of the
first utility to be acquired by the city.
Chairman Reed conducted the questioning
of several experts who were present and
in this way elicited much valuable infor
mation. The report of the Board of Pub
lic Works on the subject, submitted sev
eral weeks ago, was read, after which an
invitation was extended to those present
to give their views on the matter.
City Engineer Grunsky Informed the
committee that the investigation into the
estimate of the cost of supplying munici
pal water twenty years ago did not go
into the details contemplated In the pres
ent inquiry, but contained much valuable
Information. The contemplated plans in
cluded supplies from Clear Lake, Feather
liiver. American River. Lake Tahoe, Blue
Lakes and San Joaquin River.
Commissioner Hanson of the Board of
Works, was of the opinion that any source
of water supply for municipal acquisition
should be capable of supplying at least
Â£.0.000.000 gallons of water. The ultimate
right of acquiring two sources of water
supply should also be considered. Any un
derground supply Is liable In time to be
exhausted. Mr. Manson did not believe
that the proposition of municipal owner
ship could be submitted to the people by
a year from next July. He said he thought
that some of the other utilities could be
taken up first with better and quicker
results. If the Board of Supervisors would
issue a call for a telephone service the
plan could be carried out much more
quickly than the water supply system. An
electric light plant could also be acquired
in much less time than the water project.
Mayor Phelan thought the water supply
was desirable to take up first, because the
sources are being rapidly absorbed and
every year they are becoming more vaju
able and harder to acquire. He thought
that the idea of furnishing cheaper water
to consumers was very important.
Reed said that the municipal ownership
of telephones was especially, desirable.
The local corporation was In receipt of a
larger income on a smaller investment
than any other concern. He deprecated
the fact that the telephone companies had
been omitted in the charter relative to
the supervision and fixing of rates by the
city.
BICYCLES ON YUKON.
Record-Breaking Trips Made Be
tween Dawson and Circle City.
Special Dispatch to The Call
TACOMA. March 13.â Dawson advlcea
received to-day state that some astonish
ing trips are being made by bicyclists on
the Ynkon River. Several have made tho
greater part of the trip between Bennett
and Dawson on wheels. F. C. Smith, the
United States mail carrier, made the
round trip between Dawson and Circle
City on his wheel in eighteen days, lie
carried considerable mail each way and
made several stops. The record breaking
bicycle trip was made early in February
by Morris Levy of Dawson. who started
out light to see how quick he could reach
Circle City. He covered the 365 miles In
three days and four hours, riding over
100 miles each day. He reports the icÂ«j
path in splendid condition between these
points and says that stampeders to Nome
would stand a. much better chance of get
ting through if equipped with wheels. His
glowing reports caused people starting for
Nome to buy up all the available bicycle*.
Two women were numbered among
cyclists starting down the river.
F. H. Nagle and Robert McÃae are the
latest men xo reach Dawson from Cipe
Nome. They started on January 6 and
made the trip in fifty-two days, eighteen
of which were spent in resting and await
ing for storms to pass. They brought out
messages for J. C. Kimball & Co. of San
Francisco and will soon arrive outbound
for California. They were able to find
food and shelter every day except one on
the journey.

Clash Easily Averted.
PRESCOTT. Ariz., March 15.â J. D.
Hojick, the Tonto Basin sheep man, was
in 'Prescott to-day, having just returned
from the scene of anticipated trouble be
tween sheep men and cattle men. He
states that while his sheep were driven
back about two miles no damage resulted.
On his arrival he had a conference with
the cow men and an amicable agreement
was arrived at, the phecp men agreeing
to occupy the range on one side of the
river and the cow men on the other.
With the observance of this agreement,
he says, there is no prospect of trouble.

CONTRA COSTA
GETS ALAMEDA'S
WATER SUPPLY
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1118 Broadway, March 15.
The Artesian Water Company of Ala
meda was finally absorbed to-day by tho
Contra Costa Water Company of this city,
and the deeds and mortgages involving
the transfer were filed. The figures are
not named In the documents, but the rev
enue stamps thereon indicate a valuation
of 5750,000.
There were three documents necessary
to make the transfer perfect. While there
was a corporation under the name of the
Artesian Water Company, it was hardly
more than the personal holding of Captain
R. R. Thompson. Captain Thompson had
never transferred to the water company
much of the property which the company
operated, and in order to make the trans
fer perfect it was found necessary to oeed
a large amount of property from Captain
Thompson to the Artesian Water Com
pany. The water company in turn deeded
all of the property which it held .Â»nd
which it received from Captain Thompson
to the Contra Costa Water Company.
The property transferred consists of
three tracts of artesian well land at Fitch
burg, aggregating 350 acres, on which
thftre are ninety-seven wells, half a dozen
pumps, reservoirs, pipe and engines. I.i
Alameda there is the old pumping plant
and wel'.s on High street, the lot and
building and reservoir on Park street and
a large lot on Santa Clara avrnue. 330,400
feet of pipe. 3037 meters. 247 hydrants and
the franchises, rights and privileges that
have been granted by the town and
county.
There was filed a mortgage to the Union
Trust Company for JGOO.OOO in the f>hap<; of
600 bonds of $1000 each, covering the prop
erty of the company.
This means that the Contra Costa Water
Company has finally absorbed the Ala
meda city water supply. The deal vUt'i
the Alameda Water Company of Berkeley
Is still on. but negotiations have not yet
been finally closed. When this is com
pleted the Contra Costa Water Company
will control every water right and water
company from San Loandro on the south
to the county line on the north.

BERKELEY, March 15.â The Berke
ley "co-ed" has decided to promote
the success of "university week"
at the Grand Opera-house In San
Francisco, and she will lend her tal
ents, work and support to the events
to be then and there given. For the
first time, the Berkeley "co-ed" "*lll
take part in a public . performance â¢on
the stage, but It will only be done be
cause It will be for the interests of the
university.
When "university week" was decided
upon and a production of R. W. Tuily's
famous junior farce selected as one of
the events there was a decided ques
tion as to whether the young ladies of
the cast would take part in the public
production. It was one thing to walk
the boards for a single afternoon be
fore an audience of their college mates,
and quite another to appear before a
general audience and have their name*
appear upon bill boards and In adver
tisements.
But the production of the play de
manded the participation of the young
ladles and the success of "university
week" required the production of the
play. The possibilities of getting the
track team East made "university
week" necessary, so when this list of
demands was presented to the young
ladies they decided that they would, in
the interests of the university, make
Just this appearance before the mis
cellaneous public. But chaperon 3
were demanded on the stage, and this
has been agreed to. It may make the
stage audience large, but the demand
had to be met, and Stage Manager
Tully consented.
The farce was pronounced . by tho
university critics as the best that had
ever been prepared In that lnstituion.
It has been slightly modified In order to
make it' more pleasing to the general
public, and it will be presented by the
following cast:
Tom Harrington. James Bennett Southard;
Reginald Black. Rea Hanna: James Wob
berts. Richard W. Tully; William Everett
James, Milton Schwartz; Byron Harrlnz
ton. Ralph Fisher; Dan Davenant. John
W. Butler: Professor Smith. Charles D.
Cobb; Nugata, Demetrius Gortienker; Mar
lan Dav*nant. Miss Jessie Bohal); Dulcle
Harrinftton. Miss Ella Viola Pattfanl; Mrs.

The Swami Will Lecture in Oakland.
The Swami Vivekananda of Calcutta,
India, will give three more lectures at
Wendte Hall, Fourteenth and Castro
streets, and the Unitarians of this city
are striving to make the series even more
successful than was the last given.
The first lecture Is for Monday, evening
next. The subject chosen is "India and
Its People." The Woman's Alliance is
especially interested in the Buccess of the
addresses given by the Indian lecturer.

Estate Ordered Sold.
United States Circuit Judge Morrow
yesterday handed down a decree in the
case of Alice D. Gauld and James G.
Gauld vs. Isabella M. Romaine, the Co
lumbus Savings and Loan Society and
others, being- a suit for the partition of
an estate. The court found that Alice D
Gauld was entitled to one-twelfth of the
estate, Isabella Â¦ M. Romaine one-twelfth
Isabella Van Winkle six-twelfths and"
Lawrence E. Van Winkle four-twelfths,
and that the bank holds a mortgage on
eleven-twelfths of the property for $35 900
at 6 per cent. Henry P. Sonntag was ap
pointed to sell the property.

blows. '

ALAMEDA ADVERTISEMENTS.
BAIXROAD TEAVZL
ONE FAVORITE
OF SEVEN WON
AT TANFORAN
Defeat of Constellator by So
cialist, the Surprise of
the Day.
Handicap Easy for The Fretter â Afa
mada Led Out by Flamoraâ M.
T. Tarpey Outgamed Com
bermere in First Event.
?
Six . stanchly backed favorites wert
brushed off the earth at Tanforan yester
day as easily as a Chinese Bervant would
dust flies off the celling with an imported
bunch of feathers. Most of them received
the right sort of backing, and it could not
be said their downfall was due to any
unhealthy foreign influence.
No doubt the spiciest thing of the a'ter
noon was the defeat of the plunged on 9
to 20 choice Constellator. Tne colt hap
pened to be one of three starters in the
mile purse run. Piggott on Socialist went
out and took hold of him from the jump,
until when the stretch was reached the
three-year-old was dead. Socialist then
galloped in first, and what lamentations
his victory did cause in certain quarters.
Corrigan's two-year-old M. F. "Tarpey
outgamed Combermere in the opening
scramble. The latter ruled a pronounced
favorite, and after leading to the paddock
began to "squawk," losing out by a head.
Piggott furnished a perfect ride on the
winner. Lucidla ran a close third.
W. R. Larzalere's sprinter Afamada
dosed a slight favorite over Flamora for
the six-furlong selling number and met
defeat. Thorpe, who straddled the first
choice, got his mount away in the lead,
showing first until a sixteenth from the
stand. Here Jack Martin on, Flamora
drew up on equal terms and in a driving
finish earned the verdict by a head in 1:14.
Show honors fell to Olinthus.
The mile handicap was only a romp for
The Fretter. The track just suited the
Brutus colt, and he won hands down from
the Bto B favorite Ventoro. Erwin, aske.i
to concede too much weight, ran a com
fortable third.
Considering his recent form 8 to 5 looked
a most liberal quotation against Topmast
In the betting on the mile and a six
teenth affair. During the early running
Piggott took the Shields entry bock, but
when ready won as he liked from Sar
donic. Tappan led Wyoming out for the
show.
Afghan, from the Corrlgan string, re
ceived such strong backing at short odds
to win the fifth event that it looked all
over. The chestnut ran in the lead to 'the
stretch, where he blew up. Flamero, Â»n
8 to 1 shot, then downed Perseus a scÂ«nt
head.
In the last, a seven-furlong jaunt, Sister
Alice, at odds of 9 to 2, was never headed.
Gold Baron came from the rear, beating
Rosalbra for the place.
Track Notes.
Owing 1 to a typographical error the win
ning selections given by The Call yester
day were credited to the Chronicle in the
columns of the Daily Racing News.
It seems very strange that Daisy F
should have been entered in the Constel
lator race at all. The conditions werÂ»
certainly plain enough to show she was
not eligible. The only ones to be fooled
by such a mistake would be the. Eastern
poolrooms, which have no copy of the con
ditions of races.

INITIAL measures have been taken to
bring about an early meeting of the
Republican State Central Committee.
Last Wednesday Major Frank Mc-
Laughlin wired from Chicago to Judge
E. C. Hart of Sacramento advising the
latter as secretary to confer with Dr.
George C. Pardee, flm vice chairman of
the committee. Judge Hart came to this
city yesterday, and the conference as sug
gested took place. As a result of the In
terview the following notice was sent to
efech member of tlie executive committee
of the Stale Central Committee:
Headquarters Republican State Central Com
nilttee of California, room 166. Palace Hotel.
SAN FRANCISCO. March 15. 1900.
Dear Sir: The members of the executive com
raittee are requested to meet at the Palace
Hotel, room SSO, on Thursday. March 2J. at
I 30 o'clock p. m.. to lit time and place for
a meeting of the State Central Committee and
for the consideration of euch pther business
as mar proper!* come before the meeting.
Your attendance is earnestly requeued. By
requrst of Frank McUauehlln. chairman. Re
epectfully,
GEO. C. PARDEE. Vice Chairman.
E. C. HART, Secretary.
The members of the executive commit
tee are: Frank McLaughlin. Orovllle;
George C. Pardee and E. Kusbaumer,
Oakland; E. F. Preston. San Mateo; Wen
dell Euston, Paris Kllburn. Horace Davis,
P. N. Lillenthal, W. J. Dutton, Georgw
Stone, R. C. Meyers, I?aac Upham, J.
Alva Watt and J. K. Wilson, San Fran
cisco; E. C. Hart. Sacramento; James Mc-
Faxlden, Santa Ana; Frank P. Flint. H.
Z. Osborne, W. F. Parker, Los Angeles,
and A- G. Gassen. San Diego.
When the State Central Committee
meets it will issue a call for a State con
vention to chooee four delegates ax large
to the National convention called to as
semble at Philadelphia on June 19, 1900.
If there be no departure from established
custom the delegates chosen by the vari
ous Congressional districts will comprise
the State convention, but these delegates
will have no authority to nominate candi
dates for Congress. The notion enter
tained bjr many Republicans that the con
ventions chosen in Congressional districts
to select delegates to tho National con-

Sudden Death of W. M. Hood.
The man found unconscious in Golden
Gate Park early last Wednesday, morn
ing died in the City and County Hospital
yesterday and the body was brought' to
the Morgue. The remains were Identified
as those of W. M. Hood, a clva engineer
and . inventor. The deceased was about
60 years old and in very poor, circum
stances. He was a graduate of the Uni
versity of * Pennsylvania and â¢ a man of

The will of William B. Allen, who died
March 9, leaving an estate valued at $17,
000. was filed for probate yesterday. De
cedent bequeaths his entire 'estate in trust
to Robert Brotherton to sell and dispose
of the proceeds as follows: To Elizabeth
Allen, a sister, $1000; to Mrs. Sophie
Brotherton. a friend, $2SO; to Thomas Al
len, a brother. $100; to- Thomas W. Allen,
a nephew, $100; to Kate Elizabeth Allen,
a niece, $100. The residue of the estate is
bequeathed Elizabeth Allen. All of the
devisees reside in London, England.
The will of Julius Levy, who died March
8. was also filed. An estate valued at $25G0
is bequeathed to Henry Levy, a brother.

BURGLARS VISIT THE
PIONEER WAREHOUSE
They Hansack the Place and Carry-
Away, Eighteen Dozen Suits of
iiSlslv Underwear.
The police were notified yesterday that
the Pioneer Warehouse, Battery and Fil
bert streets, had been entered by burglars
during the night. Detective T. B. Gibson
and Sergeant Ellis were detailed on the
case. â¢ Â¦
They made an Investigation and discov
ered that entrance had been ' effected by
forcing the iron bars across the door and
then prying it open. In the . office they
attempted to break' open the inside door,
of the safe, the outer â¢ door being always
left open, but were not successful. They
took <5 cents out of the telephone box and
going upstairs broke open several cases
of underwear -and stole eighteen dozen
suits. They left a ''Jimmy" and an iron
bar behind them. j
.Charles Duzen. the special watchman In
the locality, told the officers that he visit
ed the warehouse at 3, 4 and 5 o'clock
yesterday morning and everything seemed
in proper, shape. :The job : is . supposed to
have been done. by amateurs.

SAN FRANCISCO.
The Mail steamer City of Peking sailed
for the Orient yesterday. She carried a
fair number of passengers and was loaded
to the guards with freight. Quite a num
ber of fair maidens from the Grand
Opera-house were down to see the big
liner sail. Among them was Miss Edna
Farrell, whom rumor says Is going to
marry Chief Officer R. : Bucknam : of the
Mail boat. Everybody knows of Miss Far
rell. and Captain Bucknam Is equally well
known among shipping men. He was for
merly master of the whalebock City of
Everett, but left that vessel to enter the
Mail Company's service. He was captain
of tho port at Panama for a year, and on
coming home on account of ill health was
appointed to the Peking.
Among the passenger* who went away
on the Peking was E. Vonck, the owner
and manager of the largest sugar planta
tion in Java. He has been all over the
world inspecting refineries and picking up
Information. As the result of his travels
he says the finest sugar manufacturing
plant in the world is at Watsonville. Cap
tain Merry, who goes to Honolulu to take
charge of the naval station at that port,
was another passenger, and Miss Me-
Clure, daughter of the chief engineer of
the Peking, will make the round Â¦ trip oa
the steamer. Â¦ :.
A blglleet of vessels is being got ready
for the Nome trade. The well found bark
Alaska is r being fitted out at Harrison
street wharf; the barkentino Catherine
Sudden at Mission-street wharf; the
steam schooner Alliance Is having a deck
house built on her at the Folsom-street
bulkhead; the schooner Volante is -ready
for freight, while the steam whalers
Thrasher, Jeanette and Mary D. Hume
are ready for an Immediate start, for the
gold fields. These three vessels will prob
ably be the first to reach Nome, and fol
lowing them very close; will be the bark
Alaska. The whalers are only carrying
a favored few, and the Alaska has nearly
all her accommodation sold. The other
vessels are'also filling up fast. .
The schooner Sophie Sutherland has
been sold to the Pacific Steam Whaling
Company. She will be overhauled and
may be sent north with supplies to Nome.

front yesterday. Philip Olsen of the
steamer Hathor had this thumb- cut off.
The hatch fell en it and took the member
off close to the palm of the hand. He was
treated by Dr. Hopper at the Harbor Hos
pital. . Jack Williams, 'a 'longshoreman,
got into a fight with an Italian over the
war in South Africa. Williams, who is an
Irishman, sided with the Boers, and. in
consequence Dr. Dray had to patch up the
side of his face, mend his index . finger
and â¢ sew up a four-inch scalp wound . at
the Harbor Hospital. .

CARRIE C. SANKEY'S
ESTATE IN PROBATE
Her Death Recalls the Interesting
Litigation by Which She Re
. covered a Large Fortune. â¢ ;
Public Administrator Boland has applied
for letters of administration upon the
estate of .Carrie C Sankey, who died sud
denly at the home of K. A. Lundstrom on
Tuesday afternoon. A few Â¦ months ago
Miss Sankey was the plaintiff in a sensa
tional suit brought to compel Mr. and
Mrs. Charles Coggins to reconvey prop
erty valued at $40,000 which she had deed
ed to them under undue influence. The
trial of the case lasted several weeks and
Miss Sankey was the victor. It was
proved that in addition to conveying the
property in question Miss Sankey also
bore the major portion of the expenses of
the Coggins household and furnished the
defendants with other than necessities.
Coggins was Miss Sankey's guardian, and
he contested her suit with vigor, but with
out avail. The reconveyance of the prop
erties in question had not been completed
when Miss Sankey was stricken with
heart disease and died. Public Adminis
trator Boland will now take charge of
her estate and transfer the fruits of her
legal victory to her heirs-at-law, as it is
understood she died intestate. Miss San
key was 29 years of age at the time of her
death. Â¦ Â¦
Heart Disease Caused Death.
The autopsy made last Wednesday night
by Morgue Surgeon Leland upon the body
or Miss Caroline Sankey, who died sud
denly last Tuesday . afternoon, revealed
heart dilation as the cause of death. Ow
ing to the fact that the deceased was only
29 years old and apparently a healthy and
vigorous woman It was thought best to
submit the h stomach for examination
to the city, chemist. That official reported
unofficially that he had not yet finished
his examination and chemical analysis,
but that as far as he had gone he had
found no indications of poison.

ADVERTISING IN
PUBLIC PUCES
IS IN DISFAVOR
Supervisors' Committee Will
Approve Submitted
Ordinances.
Legality of the Measures to Be De
termined â Unsightly and Gro
tesque Signs Under the
Ban.
? The Supervisors' Street Committee yes
terday took up the proposition to regu
late advertising on fences and public
places. T. T. Williams submitted two
ordinances to cover the existing abuses.
The first ordinance presented provides
that it shall be unlawful for any person
or corporation to build or maintain any
fence or frame of wood covered with can
vas or other inflammable material within
the limits of the city and county without
first obtaining a permit of the Fire War
dens; that no permit shall be given for the
maintenance of such fences over eight feet
in height nor if 'they constitute a menace
to life or property or are liable to Inter
fere with the work of the Fire Depart
ment in extinguishing fires, of which the
Fire Warden shall be the sole judge. The
ordinance also provides that such fences
are declared a public nuisance, which
must be abated forthwith by the Chief of
Police and Fire Department.
The second ordinance is similar to the
first, but is intended to regulate the build*
ing of fences around any vacant lot or
portion thereof, and limits their height to
ten feet.
Replying to a question . of Supervisor
Connor, Mr. Williams said that the meas
ures were sweeping in character and will
prohibit electric signs in front of thea
ters.
"The newspapers," said Mr. Williams,
"are willing to take in every sign and
billboard they now use for advertising
purposes in the, city. The abuse of elec
tric advertising signs was evidenced j by,
the great trouble the firemen experienced
in New York City. The newspapers em
ploy a large number of men, pay high
wages, and their interests should be con
sidered along with the claims of advertls-.
ing companies. The theaters are a nui
sance in their advertising, for they send
out a score of men on a dark night to
paste posters all over the. town. Regard
ing the claim of advertisers that they are
heavy taxpayers, Owens & Varney have
no real estate and pay taxes on personal
property assessed at $1400. Slebe & Green's
personal' property is assessed for $1175.
The ordinance aims to cover cases where,
large and unsightly signs are placed in
front of buildings.
A. Dijeau and E. L. Clapp of the San
Francisco Labor Council opposed the ordl
naces, claiming they would deny, labor
unions the privilege of advertising their
meetings and notices of boycotts on
wagons. They contended it would.be but
fair to deny the right to every business
man to advertise on the wagons he owns.
. Supervisor Maguire stated that the com
mittee desired to adopt an ordinance that
Â¦would be uniform and result in the re
moval of all unsightly advertisements. The
two ordinances presented by Mr. Williams
and another directed at the grotesque ad
vertising features now in vogue seemed
to meet with the approval of the mem
bers. They, were rsferred to the Judiciary
Committee to determine their legality.

UNEXPECTED DEATH
OF OFFICER ALLEN
Was Only Twenty-Eight Tears Old
and the Sole Support of a
Widowed Mother.
Officer C. A. Allen of the California
street police station died yesterday in St.
Luke's Hospital of typhoid pneumonia.
Deceased was only 28 years old and had
been on the police force three years, de
tailed on the Mason street beat. He was
taken sick only, a few days ago and his
death Is a surprise to his friends. He was
a very popular officer and his reputation
was of the best. His sudden taking away
has been rendered all the sadder because
he was the sole support of his widowed
mother, with whom he resided at 1141
Point Lobos avenue.

Last evening the executive committee
of the Republican county committee held
a meeting. A resolution recently pub
lished In The Call relating to joint action
by the Republican and Democratic county
committees relative to holding the pri
mary elections of both political parties
en the same day was considered. After
discussion the resolution was referred to
Alfred Bouvler, chairman, and James A.
Wilson, secretary of the county commit
tee; I. J. Truman Jr., chairman, and J. C.
Zellerbach and D. McDonald of the execu
tive committee. They will confer with
representatives of the Democratic party.

inating candidates for Congress is alto
gether erroneous.
Nominating conventions to assemble in
September will be chosen in August, ac
cording to the provisions of the primary
election law. Candidates for Presidential
Electors, Representatives In Congress and
Senators and Assemblymen of the State
Legislature cannot be placed en the tick
et unless they are nominated by conven
tions chosen as the primary election law
designates.
The merits and defects of Congressional
aspirants Â¦will not be considered In the
State convention or in the Congressional
District conventions chosen to send dele
gates to the national convention. The only
party duty that the early conventions can
perform is that of choosing delegates to
the Philadelphia convention.
In the Third District the friends of Met
calf and Dalton, the rival aspirants for
the Congressional nomination on the Re
publican ticket, agree that Dr. George C.
Pardee should be one of the delegates at
large from California. There is no indi
cation in any district of a desire on the
part of Republicans to bring the Congres
sional contest into the conventions chosen
to select delegates to the national conven
tion- On the other hand, there is an ex-

/HE SAI* FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, MARCH 16, 1900.
C. P. HUHTINGTON
PARTS WITH HIS
PULLMAN SHARES
Southern Pacific Is Paid a
Big Sum for Its
Interests.
Cars Will Be Leased in the Future on
a Mileage Schedule and Not
Bun Jointly as Here
tofore. ;
A deal has Just been consummated In
New York between C. P. Huntlhgton and
the .Pullman Palace Car Company by
which the latter comes Into absolute con
trol of all the Pullman cars used on the
Southern Pacific Company's lines. Â¦ ,
This" change, which has been rumored
for the last year and a half, involves the
transfer to the Pullman Car Company of
the three-fourths interest in the Pullman
cars used on the Southern Pacific tracks.
Previous to ISS3 the Southern Pacific
Company ran Its own sleeping cars under
the name of the Silver palace cars. In
that year a deal was entered into between
them and the Pullman people by which
the Southern Pacific agreed to sub
stitute Pullmans for their old â¢ car
service on the condition of being
â¢allowed to own a- three-fourths in
terest in all the Pullman rolling stock
used on their roads. They paid three
fourths of the cost of the cars, the same
proportion of the expenses of running
them and divided the accruing profits at
the same ratio. This contract, which was
for fifteen -years, expired in 1898, and nego
tiations have been going on ever since be
tween the two companies for the sale of
the entire interest to one or the other of
them. Pending the culmination of these
negotiations the old contract was by mu
tual agreement continued In force.
The recent sale is the end of the matter,
and henceforth the management of the
whole business will be in the hands of the
Pullmans. The sale affects the transfer of
about seventy-five cars and .the payment
of 11,500,000.
In the future the Southern Pacific Com
pany will hire cars from the Pullman peo
ple in such numbers as they are required.
The ' conduct and entire management of
these cars will be in the hands of the
Pullman Company, who will run them in
all their 'details, merely being paid so
much by the Southern Pacific Company
for the time they are used in the transpor
tation of the latter company's passengers.
Presumably the payment for their hire
will be based on a mileage schedule.
This arrangement will benefit the trav
eling public from the fact that it will
probably result in a better class of cars
being put on the run. Heretofore it was
to the interest of both the Pullman people
and the Southern Pacific .Company to
economize as much as possible and to use
the cheapest and the oldest cars that the
traveling public would stand for. Now it
will be to the Interest of the Southern
Pacific to make their road as attractive
as any other and to get the best the Pull
mans have to offer.

The revenue cutter McCulloch sailed for
Monterey yesterday on a practice cruise.
The Norwegian steamer Leander was
delayed three days owing to the Insurance
companies objecting: to the manner In
which the cargo was stowed. The two
launches built by the Fulton Iron Wonca
for P. W. Mordln & Co. of Siberia were
the bone of contention. They were not
properly placed on deck, and the agents of
the vessel had to go to considerable ex
pense in getting the matter satisfactorily
adjusted.
All of the quarantined fleet are still off
Angel Island. The Duke of Fife will be
released on Saturday and will go to the
Presidio to discharge the remains of the
soldiers now aboard. The Gaelic is still
held because the result of the experiments
with the blood taken from the sick China
man are not yet completed. The Charles
Nelson will be released to-day and the
Centennial on Sunday. The stern-wheeler
Caroline, that went out to the Gaelic
when she came in, was also quarantined,
but she will be released to-day. These are
busy times for Dr. Klnyoun.
